INTRODUCTION TO THE ENGLISH TRANSLATION
Our attention was first drawn to the Dagon and their remarkable cosm logy in 1972 when we received Hamlet's Mill by Giorgio de Santillana and Hertha von Dechend as a Christmas presenc. Thi fa cinating work included several related diagrams and an excerpt from the introduction to Conversations with Ogotemmeli(Dieu d'eau), an account of Dogan religious ideas imparted by one of the elders to the French etbnologi t Marcel Griaule. Although our interest had been sparked, for various reasons we did not pursue the matter until everal year later when we acquired a copy of Lbe book. It left us eager for the elaborations published in a subsequent work co-authored by Marcel Griaule and Germaine Dieterlen,Le renard pole, which bad appeared in France in 1965. To our surprise we then learned that in Lhe intervening years it bad never been translated into English. We borrowed a copy of the French original from a university library in Southern Californja, and as we perused the text we became more and more convinced that a classic report of this caliber should definitely be available in the English language. Since it was evident no one had thought the enterprise worthwhile up to that time and therefore it wa doubtrul omeone would in the future, we decided to explore the possibilities of producing an English rendition ourselve . It must be said at the outset that our intentions might have remained unproductive without th help - moral financial, and otherwise - of Hugh and Ruth Harrison, who have al o continued to stand by through endless delays and setbacks. We wish here to express our deep gratitude to them both. It was at Hugh Harrison's suggestion that early in J980 the Harrison-Moffett Publishing Group was formed by the Harri ons, my husband Blair Moffett, and myself for Lhe purpose of publishing The Pale Fox. We sought a licen ing agreemenc from Germaine Dieterlen, the heirs of Marcel Griaule, and the Institut d'Ethnologie in Pari . as joint hoJders of the rights to Lhe original work. This contract was signed



14 TH.EPALEFOX
in the fall. Toward the end of that year tbe Harrison-Moffett Publishing Group was melded int0 the Continuum Foundation a non-profil communications enterprise . Translation work was begm by Stephen C. Infantino in rhe summer oI 1981. lt is beyond disput Lhat this translation of Le renard pale wil l be of value to those English-speaking scholars who are not sufficiently fluent in French lo make use of the original. However, we feel certain it will not be consigned to o hlivion in university libraries across the United States, Great Britain , Canada, and elsewhere, bul will appeal to many among the general publi c who have a specific area o( interest, such as archaeoastronomy, Black tudies, mythology, oral traditions, or com· parative religion. Additionally. The Pale Fox may find an even wider audience, for the Dagon have been in the limelight a numher of times during Lhe 'seventies. mainly in television programs. In part, Lhese centered around the '•Sirius mystery," the puzzling fact that the Dogon, with no more aid than the naked eye. have known of the existence of lhe two companions of the star Sirius - a phenomenon that has general] been explained away by attributing Degon a tronomicaJ knowledge to outside, prefera• bly recent sources.• Our translation is meant also for lhose individual
•For those interested in U1e icius enigmas, we refer here to rwo statements by Griaule and
Dietcrlen wh ich , although rarel y nu.• nLi oned, are quite r rt in en l' I. In 1931 tbey were shown a cave in llii in wh ich I.h e kanaga masks for that area wert! ~1ored . Jt is Lhe Dogon custom to carve one of these mask.~ fo r ea( h ig u.i ceremony held every sixty years a nd rel a ted to Sirius and its compan ions. At thai time. th e re were nin e masks, whi le !he r lace of three m re th at bad c rumbled to dust co uld easily be recog
nized . Thi· wo uld date the e remonies hack Lo the thirteenth century. 1··u11 S1'steme SoudanaisdeSirius, .. Journal de laSocietedes African ist es, Tome X.X, Fascic ule I. 1950 . pp. 273-94. )

INTRODUCTION TO THE ENGLISH TRANSLA TJON 15
who have been stimulated into further inquiries of theirown. lor it ffer original source material (now in a form readable by alB which not only puts the question of the Sirius system into its proper etting and perspective, but, more imp rtanl, presents th fulles Lrecording to date of the cosmology of the Dagon - their spiritual heritage of many centuries. Furc.hennore, it confronts most occidental readers with a Weltanschau1111g fundamentally different from their own. The suhtleties of thought and consistency of development of the Oogon wi dom may lend credence LO Professor G riaule's own opinion that it equals the Lraditions of the ciassjcal Medi terranean cultures. However. there is a crucial difference : the Dogan a re a lfring society; their body of myths, instead of being the static lore of an extinct people , is the most active driving force of their existence. As is so amply illustrated in The Pale Fox, every aspect of individual life and of social institutions is mocleled on their c smogony in all its phase and permutations. In contra Lt our own ociety which i almost wholly profane, the sacred being allotted a circum cribed and limited function, for the Dogon there i no chi m between the acred and the profane. As Lhe universe is God Amma·s creation, indeed is Amma, it is sacred with all it c ntain from the infinitesimal to Lhe infinite. Thu , while to most of us the grains that make up our daily bread are merely the impersonal product of agrobusiness, to the Dogon they are lives endowed wilh a soul to be preserved between the harvest and the next seeding. Allied, moreover, to the "grains" thal played a role in the creation process. they are both nourishment and sacrament. The stars and galaxies are viewed by (ew people in lhe West as more than accumulations of eh mica! compounds sweeping in their orbits through an empty space, To the Dogan they are inspirited components of a dynamic whole among which there is a constant exchange of energies; for like the ancient astronomers/astrologers, such as the Chaldeans. Chine. e, Celts, Mayas, and other Amerindians, the Dogon are scienti ts and hierophants at the same time and thus oncerned with hoth the physical and metaphysical realities of the universe. Man, in the fram ework of Dogon belief, is the microcosm. As in many other cosmogonies, his b dy act ualJy provide the imagery in which the intricate creative processes are expressed. The comparison of tbe human body as microcosm with the universe as macr cosm, is a classical



THE PALE FOX
method of teaching, because the h uman being is held to contain an expression of all the forces and energies of the solar system and the universe. lf the Dogon speak more in sexual terms than do most traditi ns, it is because they regard the reproductive organs and functions as representative of the cosmic generative and creative f rces. Because it has taken much longer to produce this book than initially projected and costs have meanwhile risen appreciably, it has be n necessary to take al l polychrome drawings accompanying the text of the original and group them in one place in this English edition . A lso, it proved to be technically and financially impossible for us to maintain the diacritical marks employed in transliteration o f the Dogon lan guage. Faced with the choice of mitting these or not being able to publish, we o pted for the former. The small percentage of readers to whom they will be of interest are referred to the French original. From the beginning it has been an important consideration with us that, through Marcel Griaule and his team of co-workers, a group of Dagon elders deliberately sought to make their systems of belief known
to Western people; we feel this translation is a direct extension o f their thrust.
Ida P. Moffett

The investigations undertaken among the Dogan since 1931 by the members of the Griaule missions have been conducted principally in the villages in the Sanga region. Until 1939, lhe numerous visilS at dill rent times of the year have allowed exploration in nearly all locations in the plateau and cliff regions: almost every village or district chief wa questioned. Since the end of Wodd War Il, these investigations have been repeated regularly every year in the Sanga region ; at different intervals in the communities of Ireli, Yougo, Arou Orosongo, Nandouli, and Songo. We herewith wish to thank all ou r Dogan interpreters, informants and collaborators listed below, incl udi ng those who passed away before the pu blication of this work. A special mention must be made of our main interpreters:
Ambara Dolo, who has worked with us since 1931. He has not only translated the statements of the informants, but also supplied a great deal of in formation that revealed a profound knowledge of Dogan religious and social structu res. He has produced and commented on very many of the ritual figures reproduced in this volume. Amadigne Doi , who has translated and collected a great deal of informati n and has participated in the collection of materials (plants, fabrics, pottery , etc.). Kogu m Dolo, presently a lieutenan t in the Army of Mali, who has taken part especially in all lhe inq uiries conducted by Marcel Griaule wilh Ogotemmeli.

INTRODUCTION1
I h · D go n occupy a territory situated at the border between Mali 111111 lpper Volta which is part of the vast primary peneplain at the bend 1111h • Niger (between 14° and 15°5 north latitude and 1°5 and 4° west h 11 •itude). Dagon country is comprised o f three regions: cliffs, plateau , 11,tl plain . Of these the clWs form the most typical and the most p11p11 lated part. I here is no pe rmanently running stream, but d uring the rainy season t hL•rc are numerous torrents, which are soon dried up. The Dogan land, 1111ated in the climatically transitional fringe be tween equatorial Africa 111d the Sahara, has a Sahelian climate, that is to say four months of rain 1tr 11 m mid-J une to mid-October) followed by a long dry season. The I)11g n have made an effort to resolve the water problem by maintaining 11i1tural reservoirs, ponds, and watering holes, and by digging wells rL•ac:hing depths of 80 meters into th e plai n. !'he region suffers from an increasing aridity, made evident by th ruJual disappearance of big lrees and carnivores. . The principal tree species of the plateau are: oro , baobab (Adamsonia digitata), ommo/u , tamarind (Tamarindus indica),pe/u, cailcidrate (Kahya negalensis) ,y ul/o, flam boyant (Parkia biglobosa),. a {Lan nea acrda),
I. N ote - W have tak e n , either e ntir ly o r in part , the gene ral fac ts making up this 1111rod uc tion from those princ ipal pu blished studie. o n th e Da gon (Masques DoJ; on. CJrgonisation sociale des Dogon. Le, Ames de.i Dagon. u ·s De vises des Dogo n, La /a ngue "'ae1e des Doion de Sanga, Les Dagon ) addiog to them the findjngs of invest igations ~· in ucted after their pu bli cation. T hese wo rks are listed in th e bibli ogra phy at the end o f lhis b ol..



26 THE PALE FOX
minu, karite (Achras park.ii), onugo,jujub tree,sene {Acacia \Faidherbia) albida) . . . Trees play an important role in the life of the Dogan, from a practical as well as a religious poin t of view. They a1·e used for construction and heating, many bear edi ble fruit, the leaves serve as fodder , etc. The wild fauna in ludes hyenas, pan thers, foxes, monkey , croc diles, reptile . nake , etc. Game is comprised of birds, pigeon , partridges, d ucks, etc., leporidae and antelopes. The ponds or watering holes supply some fi h during the dry season, especially siluridae. The sparse natural resources animal and vegetable, are not ufiicient to sustain the inha itant . The sub oil contains iron and copper, once mined by I cal industry, and black marble used for maki ng bracelets. Despite inexact census taking, due to emigration to large cities and to worsening conditions, one may estimate that nearly 250,000 Dogan inhabi t Mali and Upper Volta. They constitute demographic agglomerations of widely varying importance, forming villages and small towns. S me groups of villages are connected and placed under the authority of a religious chief (Hogon). In the plain, the Dogon - who then occupy a neighborbood sometimes live in symbiosis with the Mossi and Kouroumba, each group occupying a neigh b rbood of the same village. T here are two similar situa tions in other regions with the Bambara or lhe Songhai. The Dogon, who are farmers , main tai n special relations with th Bozo, fi shermen of the Niger, which the Dogon call sorogon: they are uni ted through a cathartic relationship or "joking ki nship ," which implies the observance of a certain num be r of prohibitions and also mu tual aid both rigorously defined.
HISTORY.
Historically, the first inhabitants of the region seem to have been tbe Kour umba , whom the Dagon call Tellem. T hey I ft many pise true
INTRODUCTION 27
ture · (dwellin s, granaries, sanctuaries), particularly in the steep and most inacces ible walls of the clif s; cemeteries, and also religiou material, e pecially wooden statuette of a remarkable esthetic quality. Like be Kouroumba whom they supplanted on the cliffs, the Dog n claim to have come from Mande. T his region, extending to the west of Bamako, is the site f the ancient Mandingo empire of Kei a, also called Mali, which dominated a great part of West Africa in the thirteenth
century. Now, simultaneously with studies conducted among the Dogan and Kouro umba, research on the neigh boring populations, among the Mandingo , Bambara Bozo, Samogo, Marka, and Fulb in particular, has r vealed the existence of an organ ization of these peoples that may be considered as international , and which may have been codified at the
time of Sundiata. The social structure of these different populations, who are united by kinshi p or marriage and , in some cases, by ommon origin, is ba ed upon analogous principles and i strengthened y an identical osmogon ic myth. The rit ual manifestations, though apparently different, reveal the same beli fs and originate from a comparable mental stru ture. In addition, within his system opposite social forms are conceived as being complementary and deliberately established - this complementarity being regarded s necessary to the functioning of the whole. This system is well known by the initiates in societies that have preserved their traditional religion as well as in those which have conve rted to lslam. The principles of the uni ty of West African societie on a very large scale - a scale exceeding the one described in the precedi ng paragraph - are expressed during a septennial ceremony we attended in April of 1954. It brings the Keita and their kin together in Kaba (Kangaba on th map) , a village situated on the Niger 100 kil meters up-river from Bamako, for the rebuilding of a sanctuary, called the '' hall of Mande." In



28 THE PALE FOX
the course of this five-day ceremony, Dyabate genealogists of Kela, traditional!_ the g,iots• of the Keita, enter the anctuary before the roof is put on and recite, each in bis turn, the myth of creation of th e world by the 0 word'" of a unique God, the mythical and then historical geneaJo--gies of the Masare or Keita and of the "families" related to them through alliances or kinship. These families developed, multiplied and nowadays, constitute "populations." Related to these "families·• (thirty in number) are not only all the groups of the Mandingo tongue, Malinke, Bambara, Dyoula or Kassonkbe, but also the majority of the populations of Senegal, the Sudan, the Ivory Coast, Togo Ghana, etc. ·'In addition. one of the geographic axes associated with the r presen· ration of thi · system is the course of the Niger from its source at Lake Debo. lts course is marked by settlements which, for the users of the route, delineate the stages of mythical, proto-historic , and historic events related to the developmen t of the "families'' mentioned above and to their territorial expansion. In each com muni ty several places by the river ide are the subject of important rituals.' 2 Such is also the case witb a certain nu mber of ther geographic locations or settlements, where sanctuaries and materials are to be found that are directly associated on the myth ical, ritual, economical, and political levels with tbe representations we have ju t summarized: at Oya, among the Mali nke and the Bozo , founders of the city; at Accra among the Gah and Fante fishermen ; at Koumassi and at Lake Bosumtwi for the smiths, who constitute a separate group , which is endogamous and of international standing. Before their migration, the Dogan in Mande represented a branch of
*The griot is the tribal oral historian. Trsl. 2. G. DIETERLEN, R esultats des M issions Griaule, p. 141. LANGUAGE.
The Dogon speak Dogo .\0, "Dogon word-language ' which borrows lrom Mantle and Gjur, but has not yet been classified for certain. Il comprises several dialects that s metimes diffe c n iderably in voca bulary and morphology . S m of these are further divided into subdi lects.9 T he most archaic are Dyamsay, spoken in the Seno Gondo plain , To mbo, spoken in ifferent a reas o f the plateau: m ttos, traditional pra ers. ritual chanl · are most often pronounced in D ·amsay. The instruction of lhe young ch ildren takes place early, beca use the Dogon, conscious of their linguistic diversity "attach great importance to the purity of the language which , for them , ison a parwit.h knowledg ." 10 There exi ·ts a ritual language, Sigi so, or ·'language f the Sigui;' which is taught to the dignitaries of 1he Society of the Ma k ,awa, a t the time when they tak office during the ceremony of th Sigui, held every ixty years. Sigf o i a poor language con taining about a quarter of the vocabulary o f Dogo so; its words seem to s1em from Gj ur and NigerCongo la nguages . The very marked contrast b tween long a nd short syU ble accentuate th e rhythmic eh racter o f Lhe language , which thus le nds itself to r ecitatio n. T he Sigui dignitaries , the olubaru are sk illed in declaiming in the Sigui lan guage the story of the creation of

MATERIAL LIFE.
On the plateau, the Dogon groups have establi bed lheir seulements
on the rocks, so as not to occupy any of the arable soil so vital to their needs.
On the cliffs, a village is often backed against the walls of a fault r in
the scree; acces u. · meLim~s difficult. On the plain, however, where arable land is not lacking, the village has been able to spread oul. The village includes the public domain. granaries for common use, togu,w or "shelters of men:· where the tribe men meet. huts for men lruali ng women at the edge of the village, altars or pubUc cuJt , well , and watering holes. They are generally composed of neighborhood whose inhabitants aTe grouped around one or more family ''houses" (ginna). Each family bas its own granaries. Members of castes, miths and shoemakers, live eilber close to the village in isolated houses or, like the other , grouped together in neighborhoods. Living quarters, granaries, and sanctuaries are made of clay brick mixed with slraw and dri din the sun; the walls are coated and smoothed ver wilh lay. A hou e is generally reclangular, ometimes flanked by semicircular rooms wilb a terrace on top. T he rooms have small dimensions and are often poorly lighted: a hole in the ceiling allows the smoke to escape. One climbs to the upper fl r by a ladder made of a large carved forked branch. Tbe granaries are identifiable by eh ,ir circular r square base; one enter through a mall opening, placed up

Mill eL and sorghum are sown in mid-June. Using hoes, the men dig shallow hol s; the women and children drop in the seeds and level the soil. Weeding takes place twice during the growing season, with the hel p of the en tire family. The harvest takes place io Oct ber. The men cut I.he stalks, which the women and children bundle and bring back to the villag . Rice is sown one month after the millet and harvested one mon th earlier. The seed are wn broadca t. Men, women and children quickly turn the soil with their hoes to bury the seeds in the ground. The field is flood d, and the rain will keep it o until the harvest. The stems a re cut with a sickle, left to dry, and later flailed in mid-air. The cultivation of the po (Digitaria exilis)• is easy and not timeco nsuming as it require little care. The set!ds are sown broadcast in to a millet field, which has been left fallow . Weeding is done after lh e prouti ng of the first shoots, which are tiny and fa ll quickly. Therefore, they mu t be fl ailed immediately after the harvest. The cultivation o f the po is the ubject of special events in the life of the Dogon, made evident by the reversal of ocial situati ns and a great freedom of language . The entire neighborhood takes part in th reaping of the field of every family head, the eldersharve ting before the young. In addition, the flai ling of th po take place at night and all the young people of bo th sexes must participate under penalty of a fi ne. O nions are cultivated on large surfac sand intended for export. They are crush d, pack d into balls, un-dried, and sold by pedd lers in the regional markets, or sent to Mopti for exportation. Certai n fields near wat r sou rc are used for market gardening, fo r condime nts (tomatoes and red peppers). and also for toba cco. T he pr d ue of ea h field is the personal property of the person who b looked after it. Arboricu ltur is hjghJy developed: pecie~hat can b used as ( od fo r people and domesLic animals fo r b uilding, nd as tool are pla nted at the edge o f the viUage wbi h they protect by their shade. No paymen t is to be made to bis parents-in-law h the husband of a vakezu who is called anakezu. "'' "The settlement of I.he family group on the land and the sharing of the so il is associat ed with the sharing of wives. Although a married woma n remains a member of her clan and of her father's ginna and enjoy a ·ertain autonomy d uring her entire life, the Dogan marriage is nevertheless solidly established: the wife, at her first pregnancy, is introduced into her husband's ginna so as to ensure th legitima y of the child she bear ." 16 'I n the past , a young woman remained in her father's home un til the birth of her third child. She then left her family to go and live with her husband, leaving her first-born with her father. Presently, this rule i no longer strictl y observed, and the woman comes t live with her husband much sooner. ·•11 Upon the death of one of the spouses, the widow or widower may

The Dogan ractice the levirate, called ke ya di '"marriage of inheritance;" its purpose is lo en ure conlinui ty of the extended family by maintaining the wife and children of the deceased in1he latter's ginna. The widow, however is free to refu e th is marriage and marry the man of her choice, even if he is of another lineage, but the thildren remain members of their father·s own lineage.
THE CLAN AND THE INSTITVTJON OF THF BINU.
The Dogan claim to be equally uni ted by a more exten ive kinship than the preceding one, which applies to members belonging t one binu, Le., a group that may include several ginna. "The institution called binu (literally: left and 1eturned) presents the characteristics of totemism: a clan, a name, a prohibition, etc. a) Related to the same totem 18 may be either the members of a large common patrilinear or patrilocal ginna (literally: big hou e) or the members of several girma. For example: in Sanga (the double village of Ogol) the descendants of the four ginna, Pamyon, Do, Guinna, and Tatda, who occupy four sectors, have Tire as their totem. Only the ginna is exogamous: a man of the Do ginna may marry a Tabda woman of the sam e clan as himself, but belonging to a different ginna.
b) AIJ members of both sexes of the same bim, ob erve the same prohibi tion. c) Married women are introduced, on a certain level, into their husband's ginna during their first pregnancy so as to ensure the legi timacy of the child they bear; but they remain m mbers of their own lineage and maintain their relation to the paternal clan. d ) The priest is the only one who bears the name of the totem. THE TRIBE, THE INSTITUTION OF THE LEBE, AND THE HOGON.
Formerly, the mouthpiece of governmen t in Dagon society was the council of patriarchs. placed under the authority of the Hogon who, as the political bead, also administered justice and main tained order through the in termed iary of his delegates, who were especially resp nsible for policing the markets: price surveillance, proper course of transactions, etc. Fi nes were the sanction and, for more serious crimes, exile, or slavery. In the event of murder the culprit was permanently excl uded from tbe group , considered dead, and his belongings were divided among his heirs. Among the Dagon theft is severely p uni bed. In the past, a thief caught in the act could be killed or led before the Hogon,
who would impose a heavy fine upon him. If the fin e was not paid the thief could b sold into slavery.
The Hogon bas remained the religious head and, by virtue of this, has great authority : he is priest of the lebe, which is to say, he is responsible for the worship rendered by all the Dogan to the mythical ancestor Lebe Serou, . acrificed beca use be had violated a prohibition, so tha t the stain that had been transmitted to the cultivated earth might be purified.

OTHER SOCIAL GROUPS.
We have seen that each individual descen ds from a lineage, togu, from a gin11a, and from a totemic cJan, binu. He also belongs to other socia l groups established by village and region, themselves placed under the authority of a religious leader, the Hogon. A is the case with the sy tern of kinship, the system of age classes and the Society of Masks give the Dogon their structural framework. Each individual is admitted or introduced ritually into these associations after circumcision, which forms one of the fundamental steps in life : th operation and succeeding rites bring about the permanent fixing of the spiritual principles I body and sex of the adolescent. His person is stabilized: he will, when the time comes, be fit for procreation. At the ame time, during the retreat he receives th e beginning of an education that wilJ later permi t him to play his role and fu lfill his functions in the heart of his society.
A GE CLASSES.
Each group of grad uated, circumcised men composes an age class ton no. The members of a Lonna must help each otherfor tbe rest of their lives. From an e nomic point of view, they area son of non-relllunerated work group, that tills the fields, repairs houses, etc., for individuals or for the community. They are thanked with offers f drink and meals. The members of the tonno sometimes maintain a dwelling, duriu, built in each neighborhood where they meet after those wod hour to which their elders are entitled. They may spend the night there and also .

I HE SOCIETY OF MASKS, AVA.
In certain regi ns of the cliffs and plateau, each circumcised man becomes a member of the ava or Society of Masks. He must carve (or hnve carved for him) and wear the mask of bis choosing in order t dunce during the funeral rites. The ava is a male association charged, among other things, with the worship rendered to the first dead - the mythical ancestor Dyongou Serou - represented by the "great mask" imina na. which is the collective property of the village. The presiding dignitaries of the ava are the olubaro. re ruited from among the irmeu puru of each li neage in lUrn at the time of the sixty-year ceremony of the Sigui. For three month these dignitarie g into retreat and receive instruction: they live in the bush in the cave that bas been as igned to the newly carved mask where they are taught by the elder . They memorize e pecially the incantations and texts in Lhe "Sigui tongue, sigi so, whkh tell , in a very abbreviated manner, the story of the creali n of the world and of the appearance of death on Earth and in the human world. Until the performance of the rites of the Sigui sixty years later it will be their duty to wor hip the ancestor Dyongou Serou through the imerrnediary of the great ma k,keeper of his spiritual principles. Next to the olubaro, in charge of the cuJI for the first dead, all men qualified to wear a mask perform a similar ervice for each man who ha

VARIOUS ASSOC.LA TlONS.
We should point out the existence of certa in institutions into which an individual may be introduced by virtue of his practical skills, for example, those including goatherds (for children), cattleherds, hunters, healers, or diviners (for adult men). Each group has its special altar upon which they collectively offer sacrifices to the supernatural powers or the ancestors supposed to further that particular activity: the sacrificed
Nommo for the cowherds , th e ancestor Dyongou Serou for the healers and hunters, the Fox for the diviners, etc.

56 THE PA LE FO
their very complex study extend beyond the framework of this account. For e xample, we have seen that , during life , one of the body soul (kik inu say) of the same sex as its bearer gives life to him, whereas the other remains in the power of the Nommo; thus the Nommo protects the man at the same time he directs a nd controls his actions. He does, howev r, end back this soul during certain rituals marking the passage from one condition to another , especially during male or female circumcisi n, marriage, and ordinatio n into some social or religious office (chiefdom or priesthood). The simultaneously physical and spiritual constitution of a person , such as has just been described, applies to the majority of men called inneu omo, "living men ." Certain individuals, charged with defined religious and social functions by virtue of their status, are called inneu puru, "impure men ." The inne pu111 is incomplete; he has seven seeds in bis clavicles instead of eight; his kikinu bummone ya of the body resides permanently in the totemic sanc tuary and does not accompany him on ce rtain occasions, as is the case with the inne omo. T his conditio n is not d ue to biological conditions of birth , but rathe r inhe rited fro m the nani (ancestor) of the particular individ ual.28 The concept of the person is the basis for the representations and rites which accompany and follow the individual's death. Their purpose and effect is to first regroup certain elements which have left the body, then to accompany them, each of them remaining afterwards in a particular place. As a matter of fact, "upon death, the constituent elements of personality separate. The nyama escapes from the body and becomes an active force which is to be directed by funerary ritual; it will be transmitted according to the rules to descendants born in the extended paternal family of the deceased, who will become their nani (ancestor). The souls too leave the body: under the Nommo's control, they will serve to advance those of the children who will be the nani (descendants) of the deceased and who will receive his life force." 29 The ancestors' cult directs itself in a precise manner to certain elements of the being's spiritual principles - the kikinu say of sex - which

' This thought makes 0 the umvers an orderly whole, where the notion of law is less present than that of pre-establish d harmony, incessantly troubled and continu· all y reordered. Each part of this entirety epitomizes the whole. There i · neither subject nor object, only th ings linked in one domain. As a result of the preceding pri nciple, the Black mind establishes a network of equivalences between all things by means of a system of sym bols which. by harmonious int~rplay and i mperceptible shifts leads fr m the harp to the craft of weaving, from the garment to the creative word from th demiurge to refuse. For it involves a sort of practical and theoretical ~etaphysics which, on the one hand, explains the universe, thus respond· mg to the innate need to understand, and, on the other hand , forms the spiritual framework of men's lives.' Jij Indeed, another feature of the way of thinking and expression of the Dogon ~ust be pointed out here. Because of the postulate establishing catego~es a~1d th: rela_tionships between them , everything is significant. everything 1s a sign m the r ality of daily life and even in dreams. Leaving no ro m f r what we call chance, every element or event is charged with meaning in relation to and in intera tion with others ~imultaneously in space and in the present and fu ture time. Actually, i; 1s not a matter here of an analysis of static facts, but rather a general underst~ndin~ of soi:1ething alive, viewed from a biological standpoint. The enttre um verse 1s moving; man on earth is in motion, and life even inside the smallest seed of grain, is in motion. ' In the religious domain, this system is linked together by the existence of elaborate myths dealing with the fundamental notion of God the history of the creation of the world, of the establishment of orde/ and ~he appearance of disorder, of a sacrifice of reparation , and of man's life ma p~pu~ated and reorganized universe. "The myth, so tanie, 'astonishing word which the Dogon consider to be 'real' history . . . constitutes here the_whole of coherent themes of creation";39 this is why , by virtue of theu coh~rence and their order of succession, they make up a "history of the umverse," aduno so tanie.

In the entirety of profane or religious material (architecture everyday objects, ri tual objects) by form, decoration, and use. ' F? r the ~ogon, as for other societies of West Africa, "the least ordmary ObJect reveals in its forms and design the conscious expression of a complex cosmog ny . .. Thus, a checkered Sudanese blanket is a ~ext in which woven designs constitute signs intentionally displayed by its_ users and understood by the initiates; a basket in tended for carrying thmgs represents, when turned upside-down , the ark on which humanity descende~ from heaven to earth, the square bottom of the object representing space and the cardinal points:'44 It is, ~f course, the same for religious material per se. The plan and propor~1ons of certain dwellings, family houses, sanctuaries, etc. , are sym~ohc. ~he form , the distribution of altars in the house, in the village, a?d m the fields, demonstrate ,as we shall see , the desire to represent the different stages of the myth on the soil, to inscribe it on the very earth where man lives and walks.

'I ht design and the parallel sequences of the myth must be linked to thl! same concepts.
One repeats th e correspo nding rite when officiating in fu nction of an epi ode of th e myth whi ch is parallel to a preceding one. An infinitely slight difference wi thin th ritual (the use of a different grain for brewing the beer. for example) alone will reveal the heart of the matter , namely, the resurrection invoked and represen ted by the fermen tation and the cult - as well as to which of the myth's personages th rite is add ressed: th e whole thing is co nveyed by and to the officiants and beneficiarie of the rite , who represent the very first ones. lt also happens that a superposition is prod uced : thus the ceremonies of the Sigu i, the primary com memoration of the revelation of the "word ," also commemorate, by adjuncti n with another sequence, the appeara nce of death in the h uman world .
In the co ur se of these rites established texts, prayers, inv cations, or the recitation and chan ting of songs are in troduced. Most of them present episodes of the myth in a very syn thetic or deliberately obscure man ner, relating one or two of its equences : all req uir a gnosti kn wl dge to be underst od in their deepest sense , i.e., the mean ing by which such invoca tions and chan ts "act" intrinsically in the rit ual. For example, the prayer called amma boy, "the name of God" recited at the time of the second weeding of the H~gon 's field, con titu;es an initiation text and is intelligible only if accompanied by commentaries.46 Gesture is an integral part of the rites: the steps of the participants, the gestures of the performers, constitute the recalling of mythical events; the spiral outli ned by the dancing of the entire social com munity around Le be's altar on the day of the sowing celebration recalls the internal spiral of the elements of the "word,'" that is to say, of li fe inside the first seed.
The center or axis of the rites, in almost every case , is the blood sacrifice. Now, for all initiated men , no matter what the place, purpose, means, officiants, or subject, every sacrifice repeats the mythical sacrifi ce of the reorgan ization of the universe, the subject of the main

The position of the 111•11res incticat s systematically whether they have to do with mythical 1 ·nts having taken place in the "sky" (drawn on the ceiling) or those 1,.1 ing taken place n Earth (drawn on th wall or fl o r). Thus, for lack of a c urrent system of writing, the Dagon have multiI lit:d the uses of drawing and painti ngs and with these figures have 111scribed their entire cosmogony in a du rable fashion.

INITIATION.
How is thi s knowledge taught to man? In different ways, depending 1111 Lhe people, but which stem everywhere from what one calls initiation . 11 may be collective as among the Bam bara, by the individual's progresive admission into different associati ons or societies, male or female ,1ccording to the individual's sex , to which he gains entry by successive ,h.:grees; or it may be individual as among the Fulani . Among the Da gon this knowledge is acquired in successive stages or "words more and
,17. Infra. p. l 17 and ff. See also G. DI ETER LE . Blaso,u et emblemes totemiques des Doga n. 11· 40.

more bolh explicit and complele. '48 For the boys, this begins a fter circumcision, first collectively during the retreat following the operation. At that time, the boy is introduced to the existence of a system, but in a uperficial way; he is rarely told more. Aft er his return , the circumcised boy is introduced into the communi ty life of men. He will be able to see and hear m any things, to participate in acti ities Crom which he had thus far been excluded. rom then on, he will recei ve from the elders of his linea~e or cl an - u uaUy beginning wi th his father - certain tea hings to which he must listen and which he mu t a sorb. Thi_s knowledge, "is also acquired through the individ ual's personal expenences and th rough the func tions he performs in the heart of society.' The exercise of famiJy authority or of the priesthood, the d~~rees he attains in the associations of which be is a part (bunters, divm rs, "rituaJ thi ves,' the Socie ty of Masks etc.), work together so as t? p~r mit the integration of these traditions. The individual's penetratJOn mto _one or anothe r of these groups or associations is accompanied by teachings and explanations given by the group's responsible leaders: there are esta blished texts which are commented upon, materials and re~uJar collective ceremonies, the symbolism of which is explained. In th1 ':ay the apprentice diviner will learn from his elders the deeper meanmg of _the ~estu res he performs: an entire series of the myth's sequences will e illustrated by a series of figures, d emed "instructional," drawn upon the divination tables. . Moreover, collective rites and the commentaries to which they give nse, progressively place a higher level of knowledge within the reach of everyone. Ac tually, they are often the occasion of recited prayers or reg ular inv cations, the deeper meaning of which may be revealed to anyone wishing to learn. In addition, the part of the myth (the "story of the world") having to do with any particular ceremony is commented upo n d uring su bsequent meetin gs or in conversations held in th e shelter of me n (toguna). Thus the story is told, bit by bit and always in fragments.
One of the personal tasks of the initiate is to ma ke the connec tions and final synthesis on hi own.
AcruaUy. this "story" is much too long, its m and r ings and d iffer ent
i~~lkations are much too ric h to be recounted in one si tting, or even diVId ed into periods.

Their knowledge spans l,mr degrees which are, from least to mo t important, the giri so, the ht•,me so, the bola so, an d the so dayi.
"The giri so, 'fore-word ,' is a first source of knowledge with simple , planations in which mythical personages are often disguised , their 11 lventures sim plified or fantasized , all this in seemingly unrelated parts. II deals with visi ble things and deeds, with rituals and modem materials. "The benne so, 'side-word,' incl udes th e 'word which were in the giri 10 ' and the deeper explanation of certain parts of the ri tes and



70 THE PALE FOX
representations. Its coordination appears onJy with.in the greater di visions of knowledge, which remain partly unrevealed. '·The bolo so, 'back-word,' completes the preceding knowledge on the one band, and furnishes syntheses applicable to greater parts of the whole on the other hand. ll does not, however, contain the very secret parts. "The so dayi, 'clear-word,' concerns itself with the edifi e of knowledge in its ordered complex.ity."4 9 But initiation is not only an accumulation of knowledge nor even a philosophy, a manner of thinking. It is of an educational natu re , fo r it forms or model the individual at the same time he is assimilating the knowledge it offers. But it is still more than this Lhrough its vital character; by making the structures of the universe understood, it progressively lead the initiate to a way of life as conscious and complete as possible, in nature and within his society , in the world as it was conceived and organized by God. We feel that ano ther aspect [ the effects of the instruction shou ld be considered: it terns from a psychology of a particular nature. The Dogan myth does not relate facts merely involving adventures, rivalrie between the gods, or the effects of love and hate - love of G od, wickedness of the evil one - such as they are pres nted by o ther religions. Nor does it lead to a great detachmen t, this ideal being pr posed to man as a fin al end, a nd in view of death or of the melting into a "great whole." Rather it shows evidence of a serious examination of the very conditions of life and death ; hence , its pr ecise biologi cal aspect. Certainly, the universe is treated as a whole , but also as a living body, articulated, ordered - to the extent that even disorder has its place - fun ctional , with int rlocking parts dependent upon each other. The myth presents a construction of the universe - from that of the stellar system down to th at of the smallest grain, with man in between, himself a microcosmic image o f this world. It is from this perspective also tha t the psychological element co mes into play: hence the personalities and manner of conduct of the principal agents of the myth. The emphasis is placed upon the personage of the F ox , semper peccatus se mper Ju stus.

The Dogon had, since 1931, answered questio ns and co mme nted on rlw observation made duTing the successive expeditions from the per1' t.:tive of factual interpretation which they call "the fore-wo rd ," giri 11. that is to say, the first one they give to those wishing to lea~: ~he p1rlilicatio ns preceding the investigations of 1947 all stem from this first

In view of the perseverance manifested by Marcel G naule and his It .im; in view of the abundance of que tions whic h became m? re _and 111ore diffic ult to answer witho ut penetrating into another level; m view ,
,11' , of the desire to understand what motivated us (which remain~d far 1, 1 m satisfi ed by previous disc ussions) : in view of the fact that ob:1o ~sly tliisdesire was more important to us than any other conce rn; and tn view



72 THE PALE FOX
of the interest constantly shown by Marcel Griaule for Dogon daily l ife, observing their efforts to cultivate a difficult terrain where water was often lacking in between seasons; in view of personal relations which were not limited t? the investigation , but rather ecame mor a nd more confidential and friendly, the Dogan made a decision. We discovered this only later. The-y themselves told us about it. The patriarchs of the lineages of the double Ogol village and the principal totemic priests of the Sanga region held council and decided to instruct Marcel G riaule. For the preliminary work, they designated one of their most qualified elders - Ogotemmeli - who as is told in the introduc· tion to Dieu d 'eau,50 s licited the first meeting. The investigation lasted exactly the number of days specified in Die u d'eau, in which the ram· bling stream of information was faithfully recounted. And each day unbeknown to us - a report was made to the council on the progress of instruction. The task was all the more worthy in that the Dagon knew perfectly well that in doing lhis they were opening the door not just to thirty-three days of informatio n, but to months and years of intensive work. They never deviated from this position and we express here all our gratitude; after Ogotemmeli passed away, other initiates took his place ; after Marcel Griaule's death they persevered with the same patience and desire to perfect the job undertaken. The narrative we transcribed here stems from the interpretation of facts seen from the point of view of the "clear word ," so dayi, also called "good word," so ezu, the fourth word. For the initiate, it constitutes "the final state of knowledge (which) is only acquired through long years of application and perseverance." But this narration does not constitute the entirety of this word , because "the possession of very secret general principles and processes of calculation is not enough to say of someone that he holds the 'clear word;' he must materially know all the details involved. Thus the system of graphic signs, which includes thousands, must be theoretically possessed in its entirety and not simply k nown in its structure and its functioning." 51

74 THEPALE FO
pirit has developed and concretely realized the consequences of this ~yscem. Strictly speaking, they do not comprise variants; these are given m the text under such a heading. But they follow the indigenous development of thought based upon mythical facts. In lhe sam e vei n, we give the indigeno us e tymologies, most often spontaneously offered by the informa nt; they may have no value from a strictly linguistic point of view, but they do r eveal th Dogan mode of thought. Th~re follows a necessary intertwining of the narrativ p er se and all the different aspe ts of Dagon traditional life. Fr~m th investigator's poin t of view, the c mmentaries on signs and drawmgs are indispensable to the elaboration of a text , su h as the one ~e are presenting. So necessary these were and so natural their execution ap~ ared to the Dogon d uring the investigation, th at they themse_l es (like the ~ambara) illustrated their words with ritualisti figures, without ever bemg asked to do so. T he majority of the drawings were done on the ground or on notebooks in charcoal or pencil; the inform~t alw~ys worked by orienting himself to his drawing , thus underscoring the importance of that orientation.
The rapid evolution of West African societies leads us to believe that the Dogon, who had, up to now, kept their traditions, will adapt themselves, as have other populations, to a different type of life, established on other social, religious and economic bases. Political life , economic evolution and the increasing number of conversions - especially to Islam - brin~ ~rofound changes. The conditions of education , necessary for the evolution towa_rds modern ways of life , will no longer leave the young people enough time to enter into initiation in its traditional aspects. But the knowle~ge of the traditions of Black African societies is of great value to the history of civilization. Therefore it becomes more necessary with each passing day that they be recorded and published, in order to be preserved, taught, and written down in the heritage of the people who have lived them.

• , , ,II ton an d m rphology of the signs. Classification and multiplication of the signs. From the sign to the drawing. Represen tations. The role of the signs.
In the beginning, before all things, was Amma, God, and he rested 11p,>n nothing. "Amma's egg in a bal1"1 was closed, but made of four parts tiled "clavicles," themselves ovoid and attached, as if welded together. mma is fo ur joined clavicles; he is only these four clavicles. l t is said: \mma's four joined (sruck together) clavicles fonn (are) a ball"; and one dtls: "After that , there is nothing ,"2 which is to say, aside from that, 1111thing exjsted. This egg, in its entirety, is compared to a tem1ite hill, the base of , hich fans out int several cones;3 it simul taneously evokes unity and 111ultiplicity, for it is als said: "Amma's clavicles were stuck together; mma's four clavicles were like four eggs."4 ln their original sense, the four clavicles are also the prefiguration of he four elements, kize nay, "things four: " water (d1), air (ono), fi re (yau), 1 arth (minne): likewise, the ideal bisectors which separate them will mark the collateral directions, sibe nay, "angles fo ur ," that is to say, space. Thus, all the fundamental elemen ts and future space were prest.'tll in the morphology of the primordial "egg." Finally, the clavicles, in another manner, by their union, recall the form

FIG. I : tonu of ''Am ma s clavicles. "
The word amma means: to hold fim1ly, to embrace strongly and keep in the same place. "One calls Aroma'. name all day long, one calls him when the day begins; he is Hogon (chief) of the scheme, Hogon of wasters; Aroma arranges the scheme of things after he had wasted. Aroma one is space fourteen (-fold). To pronounce the name of Aroma is to preserve all space. The name of Amma is preservation and safe keeping of all things."7
5. amma ani guyo tonu. Made with porridge ofyupilu and ara geu under the altar of Amma called tenu amma, at the time its foundation is laid. Regarding this altar, cf. infra. p. 357. This figure is also drawn every year inyu pilu porridge at the sowing feast , on the west side of the altar to Amma called ka amma {cf. also p. 354). This altar , located in Upper Ogol, belongs to the Dyon of the Sanga region : to this grnup , it represents the altar to Amma consecrated for the entire Dogon people in Kani Kombole when they arrived in this region from Mande (cf. supra, p. 29). Altars consecrated to Amma (altars of the ginna, binu, weavers , roads, etc.) are composed of a vertically placed stone . wrapped in a mass of clay which is given an ovoid form . The stone and earth are collected at the edge of a pond. The clay contains certain elements related to the attribution of the altar: hence, the clay o f the amma of the gi1111a contains all varieties of cereal grains (PI V and VI ). 6. yu yege r•omo amma ani guyo munu.

THEPALE FO lu i:h fi traced figur es in space .
. ~ma's egg is represented in the form of an o blong p icture covered wrth s:igns, called "womb of aUworld signs''1! (fig. 2), the center of which is t_he umb il icus. From the meeting point of the two axes extended two imersecti ng signs, forming bisectors marking the four cardinal directions. Each of th~ fo u~ sectors Lh us formed contained originally eight drawings, each of which, m turn , prod uced eight more. Thus , the oval contained 8 x 8 x 4, that is 256outlines, to which ere added 8 (2 per semi-axis) and 2 for the center. The total was then 266 "signs of Aroma" (amma bummo).~ . An element_is attributed to each sector. Counter-clockwise, beginnmg from the nght lower sector, they are: earth, fire , water, and air. The two centr~l- signs at the intersection of the axes are the "guide-signs," bummo gm (hterally, "eye signs") ; the four pairs placed in the four sectors are _called bum mo ogo, "master-signs'';10 the 256 signs are "the
complete signs of the world" (aduno l(f?a bumm o). All these signs as a whole are also called ''invisible Aroma."
This hie~archy of fig ures that composes the cen tral picture is in hannony with the ''descen t and extension" of the world. It bears the ? ame of ·'articulated (organized) signs of the world in descent," 11 ind icating that each of the three categories performs a particular funct io n in

b) The second of the Lwo "guides" is called "sign of the envelope'' (kogo bummo). It is made with a simple vertical line representi ng the envelop (kogo)20 of beings (fig. 3, to the left). Its role is to bring over to the "master signs," repositories of souls and force , the exuviae of the "four elements" used in the mixture determined by th e fi rst guide. The exu viae are the testimony of existing things; they remain inside of Amma and recall that in the beginning Amma first created his own twin , that is , the universe itself. Just as the universe is the replica of Amma and contains him , this universe was - and will remain - contained by Amma in the form of signs. c) The pairs of "master-signs" assigned to each sector (fig. 4) relate to the corresponding elements of which they represent two principal states. - In A, we see an obliquely laid arc with a straight segment on the bottom forming a hook. into existence: "Aromas signs, whicb be sent int o the world , wenr , em red into things which {at that momeat) became." H But if the sign precedes the thing ignified, it is dependent upon the co nscious and active mind . It is said, "Amma , io beginning things, chose the bum m o with thought. The first design, it is through (the work of} thought that it was divided (into fo ur). It is (also by) this that the final design (in fo ur parts) was made."25 It is the mind which conceived and prod_uced the initial design and which perfected it by dividing it, so as to specify the essence of things. In its first state, the sign is an articulated whole, th en divided into fo ur parts, permitting the recognition of the basic elements which give rise to the thing. But a thing, in turn, is a ~earticulation of the parts forming a complete and unique whole, which 1s the thing itself: "The sign of Amma is one (whole). (Amma) broke it
down (into) disti nct (parts), he presented the image of the four elements {the thing) existed {by forming) a whole. "26 '
And having sprung into existence, the thing becomes conscious of itself, "comprehends itself,"27 as indicated by the presence of the kikinu say, "intelligent soul, ' in the sign. It does all th e more s in that the purpose of the decomposition was to go into the details of a defini tion ~ore expl.idtly by the separate elements than by the total sign the;
fi nally form.
All of the "complete signs" undergo the transformations and elaborations presented above. They are the developmen t of a family comparable to that of man and mythical ancestors: the initial pair at the center is the pair of great Nommo. The eight masters are the eight ancestors; the others are descendants of the first ten, and they form a set of nani, that is, of relays supporting the spiri tual principles of the first. Moreover, in the formation itself of the abstract signs that prefigure the world the very essence is manifest of this creation that is about to be

. Th yala, on the contrary, brings into play two complementary y different elements:
1. By a dotted )jne - drawing it conno tes the theoretical design of th

2. The dotted line is number, and this number corresponds to th fund amental numerical c lassification of the universal elements. In th way, the dotted line classifies the thing: - T he ya/a of the house indica tes the corners of the fut ure dwellin the supports for the structure. It i m ade of twelve dots, the numb attributed to uncultivated land and to the Fox. - T he yala of Amma's "egg" features within it a spiral indicating th form of the development of life inside the ''egg." It is made of 266 do standing for the 266 fundamental signs.44 The tonu is a diagram which c onnotes the being in the process o formation, focusing upon the organs or elemen ts essential to that being The tonu of the nommo anagonno co nnotes: a) its internal organs at the rough draft stage, b) the "putting into place' of these elements.4~
T he toy is a drawing which attains maximum realism. "toymu (drawin , and tonu (figu re) are not alike. The drawing resembles the thing (repr sented) and the figure is the diagram of the image (symbol) of the thing.',. The independence and autonomy of the sign in relation to the drawin representing the formed being are also emphasized : 'The sign is and walk about in the mind and the head. The words of the drawing are in th body. The word (of) that which is painted is in the joints. The sign is th drawing that walks about."47 And again: "The sign of the Dogon (spoken word represents things. The sign is things that move about in the world. The sign is the thing of all men. Commerce makes things go around in the world. The sign and commerce are one thing, just one word."48

the altars at the time of their fo unding, or inside the sanctuaries wh none except the priest responsible may penetrate. On the other ha the toy drawings representing the realized th ing (which has) "emer 1 from the bosom ,' are made on the fac;:ades of dwellings or sanctuan and may be seen by all. In addition, the drawing is washed by the ra1 which "carries along (to the outside )" its fonn and force to "give it man" and to promote that which it r presents into reality. Example: the first year of the construction of a totemic sanctuary, t priest hi mself draws the po with the p rridge of po pilu inside th building. The second year, the po is drawn on the outside by th sacrificer ; the rain ater "carries down the drawing to the fields," whc it promotes growth. This idea of the specific action of the sign or drawing in the future als applies to the lines traced on the ground for divination on the tables 1 the Fox. It is said: "The things which one draws, one draws the drawin 1 in order to know the things which will come tomorrow (that is, in th future) ." 1 Finally , the graphic designs contain a teaching: the abstract sign executed in a profane manner , but in secret (in the image of the "secret' of God's bosom where it was formed) , is done for the initiate · the actual drawing, which all may see, is for the neophyte. For they form a system of archives. "The signs of things of the past teach the children; the sign. of things of the past, that is the road one follows; it is so that the children will take again (re-make) the signs of the old things (customs) that on draws them."52 The more signs a man possesses the more learned he is; the knowledge of the elements of creation consists not only of the knowledge of the sign , but of the elements that compose it. Yet no one would know how to invent a bummo, nor how to modify the traditional set of bummo. To trace a new sign would be to create a new thing, thus tog "further" than Amma. One would say of a person acting this way: "He has surpassed (lacked respect for) Aroma ," amma galay. 53

The entire set of drawings of all the sanctuaries drawn in a single represents, in mythical time. ''Amma's work o[ one day ." "In one y the different drawings on the sanctuary, this is Aroma's work of one d The differen t drawings that one draws on the sanctuary each year, wh one arrives at sixty years, (their) entirety is the number of Amma w creates lhe world."63 So for all the totems of the Dogon people, a ll of Amma's signs th fo rmed the world are repeated over a pe riod of time, which connol the time or duration of Amma's creation. T his repetition, consider active and effectual has as its fun ction to perpetuate the being or th thing represented .64 The 266 primordial signs are executed on the platform of the Hogo of Arou during the ordination ceremonies of that dign itary. 2. FIRST GENESIS
Creation of the sene. Crealion of the ''first world .'' Destruction of th "first world."
CREATION OP THE SENE.
Having thought and then designed the world he wished to create
Arom a tried as an experiment to supe rpo e a bit of every kind or su bstance that formed a "flesh" originating from his own person. · ''dross" to which he added his saliva. Sofliy kneading it with his hands, he molded it into the form of a seed, i, (one also says "of a c hild' i); the parts that ran out between hi fingers formed "roots." The result of this first labor was the seed of the tree sene na, the fir t o f all plants. The work is remembered by one of the names given to the creator. ''Amma kneader" (amma manane ). The oval-shaped seed, comparable to a "chicken's egg" (ene talu ), had to contain four elements and the principle of all beings. It was larger than the other seeds would later be, particularly those of the cereals. It is said that in order to create the sene na, Amma 'cleared his throat,' which made earth ; his saliva became water ; he brea thed when be returned to the sky, this being fi re ; he blew bard , thi being wind. He did not mix the elements, but superposed th em :h e put down earth, then water , then fi re , then air. "Amma to reate lhe sene na, superposed things separated into fo ur ."78 It is also said that the f ur el ments of the sene are "the fo ur 'nails' of Amma's th umb a nd fi ngers." A figure, called "seat of the sene in fo ur ( lements) pu on top of each o ther when o ne bu ilds the lebe al tar of the Fox "79 represents the seed (the circle) and the four elements, deli b rately placed oil-center (fig. 12).

CREA TION OF THE "FIRST WORLD."
Beginning with the seed of the sene na and the tree, which are said to "the created things of the first world,"82 Amma formed a first univer Of this creation, carried out secretly in "Aroma's clavicles," little 1 known, for it was destroyed; only its rough outline can be told. He bega by planting a seed of Acacia Faidherbia (sene i), which had the shape o the thorn of the present tree, also compared to the hand bell ganana, • which ends in a tapered point. On the tip of this upward pointed thorn, h placed, upside down, a small cap of the same wood by way of a cover; it had a mushroom-like bulge at its pole that served as a handle. This cap was inverted in order to receive everything that was in the "sky," and then to spill it out over the "earth." On the circumference and on the inside wer the signs. He steadied it with the help of another thorn pointing downward and forming, together with the first one, an axis on which spun the cap, a symbol of space. The parts of the cap have the following meaning: tht' bulge is "the ball of the world," aduno gunnu, containing the germs of beings and things. The cap itself, which flares out fmm the top bulge, represents the "extension of the world" (aduno ginnay) (fig. 13, A). The whole thing is called "hand of the Fox,"84 because the germs, in order to be projected into the world , are drawn out as if by a "hand," which is in itself a passage; one also says that "the germs were projected by a hand." It is also said that Amma first put the things of the sene one on top of the other and in the second place the "hand" itself (fig. 13, B). This entire device represents the world standing on one foot, turning on itself, and filled with germs that are fertilized by the contact between the two thorns; the upper thorn is the male "sky," the lower one is the female "earth." Its bell shape, a symbol of the widening inherent in mul tiplication, allowed things to descend by their own weight, in order

signs denoting different stages - will be formed according to anoth technique in which Lhe elements will not be superposed, but rath mixed and blended in a continuous movement, the condition for the perfect integration. It is said of the sene k pt by Aroma , and which will find its place in th second univ rse: "The sene is the present witness of the former creation,· which is called "wo rld which has passed befo re ."90 It wi]I transmit to th "second world" the funda me ntal elements conferred upon it by Amma The sene was, and will remain , the first plant life created by Amma, since the "first world" had been realized through the intermediary of plant, in the second world the plan t will be considered just like a "person.'' A sacrifice will be performed annually on the trunk of th • ser1e na in the "field of the family house," ginr1a minne, and this sacrific will hold good for all vegetation. It is said: "The sacrifice offered (sacrificed) to the sene is (valid) for all trees; the trees are like the Nommo, like a person." 91 When animals eat the leaves of the sene, they will assimilate the nyama of the tree; their excrement will promote the growth of cereals and of all vegetati n. Men will a bsorb this force by eating the seeds of fruits and the leaves of the different plants, and will transmit it to the earth and the rocks by moving about. The aged who are nearing the end of their days will sleep on a board of sene. The gutter of dwellings will be made of sene wood; at the funeral of its owner it will be placed next to his blanket in the main square. This ritual will unite the witnesses of the former world and those of the new one, represented by the cloth, both symbols of Aroma's "word."

"97 The six yala of the "sex of the po" are considered astheyala of the "sex of the univer e; they will also be the image of the ex of the fi rst animal b ing formed in Aroma's womb , the nommo anagonno, symbol of th hum an f tus. With regard to the cereal seeds, the ix y ala prefigured the six varic· ties of po. T he si teen yala which follow are the "body of the po, "po gozu, that is, the seed itself and the plant that will sprout from it germ. The spiral formation of the yala makes it clear that the po will be "alive," for it is prefigured in movement. It denotes the formation of the po seed, symbol of"the smallest thing," as well as of the vital force that will give it an internal movement. life. In addition, the number of yala assigned to the future seed recalls the basic number of categories classifying the sum of primordial signs. In essence, through number, the po will contain the entire universe conceived in thought by Amma. r eversing their movement a second time, they will once again spin i11 direction.of the spiral inside the egg. Thus animated, they will be c11 "diagram of the space of the po become seven 'bounds of (the) pla
(Milky Way).113 Now, the termyalu ulo, designates the Milky Way<1r galaxy, which includes the entire stellar world of which the Earth is p and which tum<; in a spiral. The number seven expresses the multiplicat for as we have seen, it is the total of three, which symbolizes the m sex, and four, the female sex. Seven expresses, in this case, the qu infinite multiplication and development of the spiral-shaped stt:11 worlds to be formed by Amma which, having reversed their movem as they emerged, will spin, on the outside, in the original direction of I movement within the primordial egg. Thus, the transformation of ya into tonu marks a stage in the formation of the heavenly bodies visible and invisible - which are twins of the seeds. It prefigures spiraling worlds of stars that are to fill the universe of Amma - infini and yet measurable - when he will "open himself up."114
REPRESENTATIONS.
The second world, at this stage of its formation, had the shape of an e The ostrich egg placed on the terraces of the dwellings of Hogons as w as on those of certain totemic sanctuaries115 recalls the original fo Amma gave to the universe in the process of its creation. The ritual bonnet worn by the Hogon of Arou, religious leader of th Dagon, recalls both the 266 initial bummo of Amma's thought and th formation of the universe from the 266 yala of "Amma's egg." It is mad of the stalks of the eight basic cereals and has the shape of a bonnet with a convex top and a cylindrical body. It is composed of a basket work started from the apex in a conical spiral over the crown , then wound helically down around the cylinder. Between the six coils, paired Jin are inscribed forming a continuous grillwork. It has the same form as an utensil called a "bean bonnet," nu goro, with which Sudanese house

CREATION OF THE PO.
Thus inside the egg Amma himself was like aspirating motion , call "a celerated ball,• ogoru gurinu; then the oval po seed was create which placed itseli invisibly at the center. It is said: "When Amma broke the egg of the world and came out, whirlwi nd ro e. T he po, which is the smallest (thing), was made, invisibl at the center; the wind is Amma himself. It is the po which Amma I come ou t fir ' t. " 118 Aroma's creatjve will was located in the po, th smallest of things. Like a cen tral air bubble, it spun and scattered th particles of matter in a sonorous and luminous motion which, however rem ained ina udible and invisi ble. It was less a word than a thought. 11~ As the sene might have been in the first creation (with which Amma wa. dissatisfie-0) , in the second one the po is the image of the origin of matter. Therefore, it wiU later be forbidden for dilierent categories of men to eat it or speak about it; because ''the beginning of things is Aro ma's greatest secret." Moreove r, the po is also the image of the creator. "Amma, the creator, was not himself great (big), but of th at it is fo rbidden to sp ak; in his place (i.e., in o rder to replace himself) he

Upo n the cre atio n of the e ighth, each th seven will give up a bit of its s ubstances t wards its fo rmation, I whole o f these co ntribution being eq uivalent t one part, so that 1 eigh th will receive seven pa rts or seven substances. Just a la ter, at a more evolved stage of c reatio there had to be dryness and moisture, fertility and sterility , life an death , and als at the beginning of the world there had to be the dry p seed a nd the oval drop of the water element, the twinness of which i simul taneou ly antithetical and com plementary. Amma placed hi "word" in the po where it progres sively too k on the form of se en vibrations: "T he fi rst thing that Amm did was to put the seven words into the po. " 142 The seven words , as w • have already seen, constituted a couple of opposite sexes. The eighth word will be like the birth of a new being, begotten of the original couple; it prefigures the birth of Aroma's second universe, which will emerge from the po. The spiraling motion attributed to the "word" inside the seed is connoted by the name it has been given: the word po comes from a root meaning ''to roll up" (into a turban). Finally, the po, having reached a maximum of internal dynamism caused by the development of life , burst. And, imitating what had happened in Aroma's egg , the inner spiral then turned in the other direction. The figures denoting the formation of the seed recall these final stages. The yala of the seed is made of twenty-two yala in a spiral (twenty-two being in the "egg" the total number of the sex (six) and body (sixteen) of the po). The internal movement of germination was prefigured by rh movement in two directions, which is the movement of the po. 11 "The seed grows by turoing."144 Inside, while germinating, it ft in one direction, then, after bursting, that is , after the emergenl' germ, it spins in the other direction , in order to produce its n stalk. In addition, the explosion and release of the spiral in the ll direction produced - in the imag of the "word" which it contm the fundamental twinness of the po, as provided for by Aroma. 11 t "T he po, spinning as it came out, became twins."145 Thus this ., c reation, that of the po, displays a movement that belongs I universe. It was not enough for Amma the creator , who was a whirl himself, that his "word" had been emitted in vibrations of diH order, length, and effect, nor that their spiral formation could £1 things of the world with internal movement: it was still necessary that moving whole be churned in some manner, so that it might !at fertile. The po, coiled up around itself, will keep the "wo rd" until such t as Amrna gives it the role of setting this word free by transmitting i the whole of his creation.146 In the second created universe it will h with all the seeds born after it - evidence of the "vital" aspel·t A mma's "word."
CREA TION OF THE EIGHT SEEDS.
Continuing his work, Amma then created the other grains, dene,• beginning with the eight seeds prefigured by the forty internal yala the spiral. Like the po, they will be twins, male and female. And th movement imposed upon their creation will allow some of them to fo mutations (bibile) within the womb and produce other varieties (th emme y a, for example, producing other varieties of sorghum, the a geu producing other varieties of rice, etc.). y a mea ns "woman ," as well as "yesterday" and "to leave." A pop e tymology gives y ara, "to release" ; for one says that "the second c
tion in the past (yesterday =ya) was released and liberated by Am T his is also an allusion to the beginning of things; ya , "to leave," h tak es on the meaning of "creation by action"; the expression ya1'/ used to say "in the beginning, at the origin." Of feminine essence, the emme ya will later be the guardian of female sex souls of the cereals, while the po will be the guardian of I male souls. The emme ya is the "mother of the cereal grains." It is sa "In the family of seeds, the po is the father and the emme ya t mother. "100 In this manner the formed seed acquired the shape of the femini sex. Given its reproductive role, it was also made larger than the po. It said, figuratively, that Amma "tired himself making the emme ya se (the world) enter into the smaller po seed (the origin of the world)." But the emme ya did not burst like the po; it germinated. And i germination took place slowly, in contrast to the virile emission oft po. It gave ''birth" to all the varieties of sorghum, the first being th emme di giru. In speaking of the emme ya, one says: "It is the biggest all the emme; may it make us grow." 15 1 The figures representing the creation of the emme ya recall i prerogratives; the graphic designs connote the seed's germination and likewise, the germination of all cereal grains. 152 Just as the po pilu, th plant will be shown in its entirety (on the fac;ades of sanctuaries) b dipping a tuft of millet in rice porridge and quickly slapping it fla against the wall: there is a spattering forming the rays of the drawing an a thick plaster at the center of the ear. T herefore, the nomm o, silurus fish , origin ally existed in the form of an egg som what larger and lo nger than the p o seed . In this egg Am.ma will fi rst pla e fi re, then water that wiJJ unite with the fire. In the form of blood , th is water will enter th e fi rst clavicle to be fo rmed , just as water flows into a pond. The powe r o f the fire wiJJ increase the power of th water. Then earth will be added, tha t wi ll form the bones, but part of which the creator will keep. This earth, like the air, will be surro unded by water. Finally, the air will penetrate the formed being and endow him with life. Thus, the creation of the nommo-silurus prefigures the creation of man , who also begi ns with fire, for semen is fire in the womb. THE PALF
T hus , d espite their ap parent abstraction, the se two figures, like Dogan signs, express in adva nce the fu ndamental states and firs t sta~ the being in formation : its identification with the primordial seed, / ICJ primitive quality in relation to the womb being "form ed withoul appearance of menses''; while the next stage, almost concomil
8A
FI G. 36:ya/a of the nomm o anagonno.
brings out its androgynous quality, which will eventually manifest it.· as twinness. Henceforth, the nommo-silurus will bear these two successi and definitive physiological characteristics, which will give it its wholen and purity. Essentially "alive," omo, it will be of immortal essence Ii its creator. Sketched in the egg where Amma had placed those elements essenti to the support of the being, the nommo anagonno was slowly formed The pattern of the yala of this formation depicts the body of the siluru at the initial stage. In order to represent the emptiness of the universe a that time, each line making up the whole is egg-shaped, yet remains a empty circle : the yala of the nommo anagonno are empty circles becaus Aroma wiU put life into them.

There are figures recalling this m ultiplication. The first ne, c,t "ton u of the four nommo anagonno" (fig. 41),228 shows the desig the first four formed beings: it constitutes the tonu of the four ori • bummo of Amma's placenta. Each diagram is made of six strokes dcm,1 the y ala of the " ex" of the p o. T his figure recalls the formation of nom mo anagonno inside the po (hence the identity between the s and the fish egg), and makes it clear that Amma will proceed by f summo ning the reproductive organ. The second one, drawn on ceiling of a ritual cave representing the "sky where the eight nom anagonno were formed,'' shows this multiplicatio n in Aroma's wom representing it by the design of the eight fish heads and the vital orga (sosogu, ani guyo, ta i). It is called "tonu of the great twin nommo '' ((I 42).229 The figure is not a toy, because other events will come to up the order of things before complete realization of the eight nomm anagonno.
Meanwhile, this multiplication of the original being implied ideas wealth, good fortune , arid power that Am.ma had bestowed upon h creature, which was complete, fertile, and animated by his Wor "Amma, who brought together (or made to come together) all thin , enclosed them in the po. 228. nommo anagonno nay tonu. Drawn with yu pilu porridge mixed with sa wate r, at th four corners o f the upper granary, delu. placed above the hall , on the terrace o f th family's "big house" (ginna) when th is dwe lling is erected. This granary serves as a recepta cle for grains kept by the family head from the harvest until the next sowing. thus considered to be placed "in the sky''; the seeds therein are the repositories of the spiritual principles o( the grains, the rest of the harvest having been desacralized - thus become suitable for consumption - in the course of successive rites performed by the community (on a family and clan level). 229. nommo die dine 11 tonu.
