The fourth, which in the form of nommo anagonrw will bear the n of ogo, is not r presen ted as a fish. Indeed, in the very course 11 completion, it will rebel against its creat rand bring disorder int o universe. It will ev ntually assume the form of the pale Fox (Vul pallida), the image of its downfall; for the fo rmation of the eight per beings, upon whom Aroma had bestowed the "word ," and who wcr be the chiefs or the kings of the creation in progress, would be pc nently disrupted by the individ ual activity and by the initiatives of Fox.
6. AMMA'S WORK
Development of the "second world. "
Amma performed the work of creation in several stages. This wo consisted of giving vol ume to that explosive force he had conferred his wn thought. projected outside of himself while he was materializi the world . The animated fo rms, born according to the successive stag of the drawing a nd transmitting their germinating force from the insid of th e seeds all the way to the stars, we re only one facet of the unive : motion that Amma himself had bestowed upon the world.
AMMA 'S WORK.
Initially , "Amma's egg in a ball" (amma talu gunnu) was , as we hav seen, made of four parts that adhered to one another , of which it w said : "The four clavicles were stuck together, the four clavicles wer like four eggs." It is represented in this form by the "design of Amma' clavicles."237 Now, Amma produced his own creation by "opening (himse!O up." Aroma's decision, a prelude to his actions, is stressed by a figure made above the "egg": it shows a double door (ta dine, "twin door") , locked with the "key of the world" (aduno ta i).2.16 One of the sides of the

265. As is the case with the sirige mask, there are other variants of the kanaga mask ; on of the masks represents the insect barakamaza , which will anchor the ark of the Nomm, on Earth after his desc nt (cf. inji-a, p. 472). The other represents Ogo, turned into the Fox, flat on his back, dying with thirst , his four paws in the air to implore Amma (installment 2>. These masks are also represented by different d rawings in the caves; their details indicate what they represent. The figures dealing with t be amma ta, the sirige, and the kanaga to which we refe r in this volume, are drawn o n the walls of the totemic sanctuaries where they represent, not masks, but rather vents said to be ··of the old world ." They are considered, in mythical time, 10 be anterior to the masks which, howeve r, recall the same e vents, but in a context related to the a ppearance of death on the Earth . The fig ures, therefore, a re no t prohibited to the totemic priests, whereas the masks are. See pl. Vl l , 2.

THE PAU
Ogo st le Lhe "nerves," volu, of Aroma, that is , Lhe "nervurl· internal volu . of the egg which were eventually to open inlo clavicl es. He seized the nervure which formed the separation am.I a utensil in the form of a bo nnet with it. Starti ng artbe top a nd end 111 the bottom, be made it so welJ, in fact, that . once the object was finish he fou nd himself enclosed inside, just as Amma himself bad encl, him elf in the primordial egg. This object, later named yurugu ~" "the Fox's bonnet.' and then, to ridic ule Ogo's fai lure, nu goro, "bon of the beans," was round and egg-sha ped in the image o f "Am.ma's s '·Ogo, having stole n Amma's nerve. began the nu goro, he wove it goi down . Ogo, by spinning, wove t.h nu goro; he t urned it upside down his head ."5 Ogo had made the basket as had Aroma his creation: the two moti11 we re represented which Amma bestowed upo n the fi rst seed , a nd lal upon the two worlds : the spiraJ by the coi li ng, the vibration by the ra. T hus, Ogo had re peated the vibratory-spirali ng motion of Amm· Therefore, his work was a challenge to that of Amma. Amma then said "That r sembles the image which l have created; do not contest m ·• because, seeing him work, Amma feared that Ogo might be able t make a world just as he himself was making one. However, si tuated there under the overturned object in a pla "without shadow or light ,' since the braiding was loosely done, Ogo defi ed Amma. At th at point , irritated by this succe , Amma cut off a part of his tongue or more precisely, "the vein of his tongue ." So, Ogo was deprived of the fu ll pitch of his voice, thus of the range of sounds he was able to emit. Arnma then sent him back to the clavicle of the west and Ogo left the nu goro basket in the sou thern clavicle, where it remained.
OGO'S FIR ST DESCENT.
Like his twin brothers, Ogo had received the "word," therefore the "knowledge'' to henceforth appear in this universe: the impairment of the pitch of his voice - even if it was connected to the utterance of the word - did no t deprive Ogo of h is possession of that word . This mutilation was simply Aroma's warning.

Ogo's failure and his lost twinness. So, l:Jy these births Ogo made of his "mother," the Earth , the 1•q lent f his wife, i.e., of his ideal twin . But as this union produced incomplete, imperfect, single beings , it was unsatisfactory and sh, Ogo's first failu re in his an:emp t to eq ual Amma.
REPRESENTA TION OF OG O'S FIR ST AR K.
In the ancestors' sanctuaries in the fami ly houses is a sort of reccp cle called nu koro, "bean box," or yurugu koro, "ark of the Fox" ( 57).30 In the shape of a weaver's shuttle with high sides becoming narr at one end, it has a handle at each tip fo rming boat-stems.3 1 Wh tu rned upside down, this "ark ," with the bow higher than the ll'r evokes the fu ture silhouette of the animal which, by the way, is brand int the bottom with a red-hot iron together with the image of a po Sel' the symbol of all cultivated seeds. By it shape , the ark is also the prefigu ration o f one of the rhombus • which will be the image of a Fox that would spin when grabbed by th tail. Li ke the ark, the instrument is carved in sene wood , a tree who. avatars are related to those of Ogo. The oblong shell has notches at the bow and stern, symbolizing th degrees of Ogo's descent. It is the image of the egg of the world and o the placenta of which Ogo tore out a part, marked by the opening of th vessel, the sides of which show the direction of the "stroke of the claw.'' The buttonhole shape of this opening and the narrowing of the walls illustrate the tabors of Ogo who, wanting to act on his own, tried to close himself up in his ark. In doing so, he was doing the opposite of what would be the future task of the Nommo, who had to act for the good and the multiplication of all, as indicated by the widened shape of his own ark, sym bol of the extension of all beings. The "strokes of the claw" marked the hull with grooves which are reproduced by twelve longitudinal lines , branded with a red-hot iron,

The uses of Ogo·s ark are varied : the one placed near the an altar served in the past to measure the seeds neces ary for the dail of the family. It was u ed to scoop up the beans for seeding, reserve after the harvest. This gesture was symbolically vali I seeds. and it recalled that the bean will be the first to germinate in the holes d ug by Ogo.36 as will the po pilu in its own hole imml'd after the bean. In light.of this , to extract it by separating it from th
sand in which it is kept is like separating it from the po, which is as r, sand, i.e., to preserve it from any risk of impurity.37 The arks of the family houses, measuring a cubit long and a h wide, may be used either as a seat by turning them upside down, or platter on which one puts the sacrificial meat for the old men. How • this should only be goat's meat, because this animal, associated with Fox, is his agent: none other may be put into this receptacle. THEFT OF AMMA 'S SEEDS.
Seeing that he could not obtain what he was looking for , that is, his fe male twin, Ogo wanted to get hold of the seeds created by Amma , including the po, the cornerstone of creation. In this way, he thought he could take possession of the universe.

I n descend to the Earth, Ogo made a "hole' in the egg, which "Amma 11 111sformed into a moon, so as not to lose the testimony to the theft." I 11 •r, the "new moon" will correspond to that moment of the theft. I 11 ) m the aspect of time, the second stolen piece of placenta itself ,, presents a "month," that is, a "moon ," the one that precedes the 11wing,6] th us associating the theft of the seeds with their future crop. I hus, the two stars (the sun and them on) are the celestial evidence of 1hc placenta of the Fox. One says of th figures that represent these heavenly bodies on the fac;:ades of the totemic san tuaries: "The drawings of the sun and moon (drawn) on the sanctuary of the binu mark the placen ta of Ogo."64 While descending, he checked the stopping-points, marked during his reascent, "naming a race of beings at each one of them ." his descent is catled "winding road," ozu gonnu. The "chain" which allowed the ark to descend is the palmyra tree, siu: "The top of the tree to uched the sky, the bottom will take root in the earth and will finally rot."65

62. yurugu me gunnu kabulu nu koro polo vo sugu-le koro sibe nay vo sugu-le nay-le mona.
63. That is to say, bado. Children conceived at this time are ogo, i.e., "leaders," because this is the "month before the con ce ption of the seeds." During this month, the new moon represents the days of menstruation preceding conception. Now, since "Amma did not wish to take back the piece of placenta," the children conceived at this time may become leaders.
64. ie pilu·le nay-le binu·ne tonu ogo me yalay.
65. At this stage of the myth, the Earth is compared to the field of the joint family , ginna minne. The tree , then, belongs to the family head, ginna bana. He is the person one asks for fibers to make ba kets, some of which are given to him in thanks. Sometimes, palmyra trees are planted in the field of the ginna.

At Amma's bidding, three insects accompanied Ogo am] over him. First, the female ant key, who dug the hole fo rth • d and the termite tu. called·•Amma·s drawer of water," who helpL·tl drink. T he anl and the termite. who were at the center of Arom a's .., and his fou r clavicle , are Aroma's assistants or titiyayne. 66 They descended along the fork of the world to wat, h o, universe io formation. The ir role with regard to Ogo i.s implied h name: key. which designates the ant, comes from k ele. "to se parnll it "separated itself from Ogo"; tu, the te nnite comes from tumo, "t 1 ·1 o r ·'coun terpart,' for it acted in the same way as the ant.
T he third insect was the spider dada yurugu geze gezene (liln U dada who spins the Fox's thread): it was related ro the kikinu bum11H ana of Ogo body (whereas the minne iru keke descended with the 11 was linked to the kikinu bummone ya./. Placed on the second arl,,. which it formed the cover its head facing south, it hid Ogo fro Aroma's eyes. In the ark were also all these ds of wild plants, bom from the spinnin of the sene and which it had left in space. The male po pi/u, cornerstone of the world, directed Ogo in th desce nt. Now, the po is the symbol of the origin of b ings and of materi I goods, just as the placenta is the original envelope of man, who begin his life as small as the seed. Therefore, Ogo thought he possessed th ource of all things when he stole the one thing and the other. "One say that tb Fox stole fonio and placenta from the sk y, saying that with the · one gains a (c rea tion of) family and of material goods. '67 Ogo s mistake in seizing the po and the placenta was that he had penetrated the secret of the origins, the sec ret o f Amma himself that is to say, in short, that he had understood Aroma's essence.68 One also ays that Amma - who foresaw all things - had allowed Ogo to descend and fonn the Earth to prepare it before the arrival of man: he had entrusted him with the destiny of the world. I he face of the dommo or "rabbit" mask, in its entirety, represents lu · events: the rectangle is the piece of Ogo's placenta; it is divided 111111 two parts, the right side denoting the Yeban who escaped being , 11shed, and the lefl side the AndoumbouJou whose ex organ was ,11shed. I 1/E "SENE NA" AND THE WORK OF THE SPIDER.
When the sene na, whose seed had genninate-0 in the soil, noticed Ogo's ii ·eds, it tri d to return to the ky. lts quest is compared to Ogo's: alone 111d incomplete like him, but in an opposite way, it sought its complete11ess in the sky. T o do this, it began to turn o n its axis; its stalk, which rirst supported two late ral thorns, grew in a helix,79 while its roo ts d ug
78. For the dommo mask. cf. M. G riaule. Masques dogon s. p. 454 (also installm ent 2. I0lh ye ar) . 79. An allusion to the trunk of the sene whi ch is cove red by bark th at tw ists around it in a helical fashion ; the tree's branches grow around it in tbe same way. T his helical motion is called minne. For a twisted brac ele t. one says minemu or minemo.

into the soil. While it rose in a hel ix, its movement attracted the e s of pi that had descended with Ogo's seco nd ark and had been hurled ou tbe spin nin0 motion. The were then enve loped by the movement n sene, which grew while taking them in to its whorls. T hen it threw t out, and they fell on the earth and germinated. The fi rst seed caugh the sene was that of the mono, a wo rd meaning "to bri ng together ," the o ther plants that reach the earth later will be cl assified with the s which represen ts the tree par excellence.
Now, the spider dada y urugu geze gezene (literally: ''da daij0 who ho the thread of the Fox") had been delegated by Amma, when descended for the second time, to watch his de ds and to report them Aroma. However , since it believed that Ogo would be victorious a that, once he had stolen the po, he would take over creati n for his o advantage, it betrayed Aroma's trust. lt entered the sene na (that w beginning to grow) in order t "weave the words" of Ogo. By weavi them io the sene and thus giving a new form to the signs that Ogo h stolen wi th his pla enta , it wished to d raw in to the sene the fo elements riginally contain d in the sene seed. Placed at the center of the acacia , the spider wove its threads in conical spiral fo r the placement of the warp, a nd by moving verticall for the coming and go ing of the passing oof. 81 Through the fabric, it sifted the germs that had been hurled into th universe by the spinning. The sound of its work , of the same nature a the word, grabbed them as they passed by and, with them, fastened itself at the crossing of the threads. The spider was in the tree , weaving its thread , which it wound around the tree. The tree tried to close the insect in and became longer, first twisting its trunk, then developing its branches, which formed "four
80. dada is used 10 describe a weak or sick animal th at walks wilh difficulty. 81. The conical spiraling motion is called digilio bara vani, "circle (or turning) that increases in width ," while the helix minne is also called "similar turning," kekeu digilio. The in-and-out movement of the woof bears the same name as the spider, dada . According to a popular derivation, those terms a re to be compared to dana: "st uck," because the band rolled up and stuck very tightly to itse lf, as implied by th e repetition of dada. This last term also applies to the vibration of the loom .

T he events relating to Ogo's deeds and to the avatars of his placcn are represented on the ground by various arrangements for ritual p poses and for the instruction of the initiates. Generally speaking, the rock paintings, the structures of puddl clay, the alignment of stones, and stacks of rocks, by virtue of th symbolism attributed to them, all bear evidence of mythical events sai to be "of the old world," which are recalled and reenacted by certai rites. I II ICF AND RESURRECTION OF THE NOMMO 249
,, 11 l main with him in the "sky," and by the nommo semu on Earth. 1II hi oft.his, the design of the sacrifice of the Nomrno is represented 11 ,;i by a serie of altars delimiting a "mythical territory" where 11tlv the rites are held that symbolically repeat the act of reorganization 11 ·on equences: these rites renew its effects while again actualiz1111 Ill at the rune time. 1 ••rtain number of them are placed on an earth platform called lebe I •rrace of Ube" (cf. Pl. XIII, 3), located to the south of the village l lppcr Ogol, on which no one - except for the designated prie ts 1read and which represents the ' sky" where the sacrifice took I , , , as well as the "placen ta" of the victim. ' Another series of altars 11 nc.J tb.is system of representations to the north, first across the , ,11rs of dwellings and then beyond (fig. 76). I hese altars, different in form and in ritual usage, are asso iated: with the parts of the body the vital organs, the blood of the ictim 111d Lhe "word"; with the stages of th sacrifice a nd resurrection and with time; with different stars and with space; with the mythical ancestors, the totemic clans, and social organization. fhe arrangement outlines a schematic representation of three Liturgi11 calendars (solar, Venusian and Sirian), which are combined with t he urrent lunar calendar for determining the various dates of agrarian, I unily, clan , or general rites. T hey are placed a nd consecrated to record on the ground and to ,·nmmem o ra te : - the execution and resurrection of the Nommo in the sky and before the descent of man on Earth; - the execution and resurrection of Lebe Serou,one of the eponymous rncestors , wb.ich will take place on Earth the second year after the descent of man; - a third sacrifice - commemorative of the preceding ones - will he pe rformed the fifth year after the descent of man on the Earth to consecrate the altars which , theoretically, were built at this stage of the myth . The immolation of the victim is followed by his consumption , which constitutes an alimentary communion for the entire totemic clan
I. Every year, tbe Hogon 's mille t from the fi eld of Lebeis cru shed on this terrace. "Th e mill et of the Hog n that one cru ·be o n the lebe data. the te be data is lik e th e sky" (ogo ne yu /ebe dalane du11oni, lebe dala alaga/a a11ay ).

IMA 'S CHOICE OF THE VICTIM AND THE SACRIFICER.
ht:n Aroma had ordered the nom mo anagonno titiyayne to purify 1h, l:.arth , one of the nommo anagonno, formed in the same part of the l1111hle placenta as Ogo claimed that this should be his prerogative. 111ma then designated him as the victim of the sacrifice. Comments on ,his choi e emphasize that the nommo anagonno in question had revolted 1 •ai n t Aroma's order, saying thaf the nom mo titiyay ne had already 1 ·ted by crushing Ogo's placenta , and that he himself had played no role 11 all. Amma then ordered the nommo titiy ay ne to sacrifice him , thus h wing the permanent authority he had over his creatures by asking of 1hem the obedience Ogo had refused him. According to a similar version, Amma gave this order to the nommo nnagonno die who, in turn , delegated his sacrificer, the nommo anagonno tiliyay ne, to perform the operation on the third , Ogo's twin brother. Another, more aberrant version of the choice of the victim reflects the alert and sly personality of Ogo: according to this version , Amma first wanted to sacrifice Ogo, but Ogo argued that, through the divination, he would later be of great service to mankind and therefore it would be better to choose his twin brother to purify the world.

I' \IU TION OF THE SOULS OF THE VIC TIM.
I It • sac rifice of the nommo anago nno, known from lhat time on , ,mmo semu (sacrificed ,wm m o), lasted for e igh t periods. II ·fore performing any o ther work, Arnma star ted to divide the fo ur 1,rnly souls o f the nommo cmagonn o into two , th us creating four up plementary souls, which will be given to him later and will be his "sex 1111ls," or du kikinu. f his preliminary deed profoundly m odified the essence of those first h ·i ngs created by Amma. At first and rogyno us in their fetal fo rm as 11-,hes, they wiU later be o f differen t sexes: sexual union will become 11 ·cessary for the propagc!tion of the species. By dividing the victim 's ~1m ls into two . Amma created the model for the separation of the sexes in general and simultaneously endowed his creatu res with those spiritu al pr inciples associated with procreation. Comments on this fu ndame ntal deed emphasize that if the nommo a11agon110 had n ot received his "sex souls " that is the image or presence of th e "ideal" female with whom he was to be uni ted later, he would not have reproduced wi th her , but rather with Ogo's fem ale twin whom A roma bad entrusted to him. Now Am ma wanted to prevent this fro m happening, since Ogo's fe male twin was tainted by his increasing



254
impurity. In addition, this un ion of twins was not to produc~ 1 I l'um. considered to be superior to those that preceded them. Amm thus protecting the nommo anagonno in advance against both a p, ,1 impurity and an "overstepping" of the fundamental rule of tw in that is an excess of which Ogo be ame Lhe living symbol. The 1111 had to c nform in e very way to the ord er establisl1ed by the crl'al Finally, llle physical identity between Ogo and the sacrificed 1111111 both twins formed in the same placenta, implied that if the lallt·1 had sexu I relation wi th the female twin of the form er , that deed wn have been considered incestuous and , f r him, would have consisll'd "consorting with himself." The importance of Aroma's splitting the spiritual principles of t sacrificed twin into two , creating a total o f eight kikinu (which will a!Jo tted to every human being), is also stressed by a commentary on th act of the Nommo. For one says that the present nommo anago1111 testimony on Earth of the p rimordial androgyny and of the propagatio o livings beings in Am.ma's womb have eight body souls and eight s souls. During p urification sacrifices perform ed ne ar the water, the pri ·s says : "Nommo, here is you r purificatio n, eight souls above , eight sou l below, togeth r ix teen."2 This versi n points to the future procreative role of the sacrificed nommo anagonno; as humanity's ancestor, he will give human being their spiritual principles and will protect them. The souls of the victim arc shared, for although he maintains his wholeness (eight above . . .), br gives part of it to human beings, his "sons" (eight below . . .). Through the intermediary of these principles he will watch over them and intervene during their lifetime. To help the victim realize his future role, Amma will then take away his teeth, in order to keep the seat of the "word" at the stage where it becomes articulate language. A commentary emphasizes the role of the teeth by specifying that the victim lost every other one, the bottom teeth fitting into the gaps between those above them, and vice-versa. EMASCULATION OF THE VICTIM.
Amma then proceeded with the emasculation of the nommo anagonno. this act bringing about a purification of the first double placenta. The victim was "sea ted," as it were, facing south, in the middle of his placenta where his umbilical cord was attached (fig. 77, A). The sacrificer. i.e.. the nommo titiyayne, folded the penis back against the cord and cut off the whole. Thus, Aroma separated the victim from his placenta and from his sex at the same time.

I hese representations are recalled at childbirth: a boy's umbilical ,,111 is pinched between two sticks (of pelu, kilena or satele) or between 1 , , shards of pottery and is cut off between the shards. ''The trace (of the blood I sacrificed Nommo. which stopped at igibie, (creat d) Ve nus oh111 The sex was empti d of its conte nts and placed by Amma in the in the place where Venus was to appear in the second position, l' "sta r of the west." donno tolo (or albana tolo) (fig. 77, H). The cord, koni, stayed in the center ; there a tiny little star emc r yazu danala tolo, "star that accompanies Venus." Thus. the first stages of the sacrifice of the nommo anagonno w ·r the origin of the formation of the stars and planets. Their presence in world of spiraling stars, prefigured by first the rolling up and then unrolling of the yala of the primordial egg and by their movemelll space triggered by Aroma's deed, will testify to the reorganization of t universe produced by the sacrifice. Amma put aside the contents of the sex of the emasculated hermap rodite fo r later use, just as he would utilize th e pieces of the victim body. This action formed on of the fundamental tages of "Amm work" with the victim 's body: his purpose, as we recall , was to retriev one by one, all the "a rticulations of the word" originally develop inside the po seed and bestowed upon the nommo anagonno. 21 By appr priating the contents of the sex, he appropriated the eighth war evidence of the po '.s germination and of the fertility originally conferred on the victim and on his twin brothers. The sperm or seed of th sacrified Nommo contained: - water, di, male and female, which are, respectively, fresh water and sea water or "salt water," neu di; - "rain hatchets," anagulo ; - convenant stones or "beads," dugoy, the images or doubles (bibile) of the first seeds; - some cowries, kele; - the image (bibile) of two anagonno sala siluri or "ordinary anagonno." These six elements have a symbolic relationship to the six yala of sex

The du1;0 is made of a perforated ova/ or round stone, studded with either an old glassware piece (co/Jeered in the bush near formerly inhabited areasj, modern beads bought at the market , or even a small iron ring. Its co/or varies greatly. The Dogon distinguish between four types of dugoy: the ogo dugoy worn by the Hogons , the binu dugoy worn by the totemic priests, the amma dugoy worn by the priests and priestesses of Amma, the nani duiov worn by individuals of both sex es. 11m• ut the fo reskin was the ransom for Ogo's tearing off th e 111 1 it will later be repeated for all men: by circumcising a human 1111 • repeats what Ogo did (on the cosmic plane) to try to gain ton of his female twin as well as what was inflicted upon him in , 111 separate him from the stolen spiritual principles and from the I 111 which had become their physical support. lu lnur sex souls recovered by the operation changed into a tree, the ,,,, r presented as a trunk with three roots. Therefore, putting this , of events into a single image, one sometimes considers Ogo as 111 "attached" to the kilena, (i.e., to the four souls he had stolen) and !111~ way undergoing the ordeal inflicted by the nommo titiyayne to 1 hack the sex souls by circumcising him . . . I o's circumcision is recalled by a series of figures. I h • first one, somewhat schematic, is called "tonu of the Fox's 1,cumcision"3~ (fig. 81). Inside an oval representing Ogo's ark, a rocket/I. red outline represents the kilena, formed by the four sex souls of the 11111,rno, under which Ogo is tied (in his future four-legged form), ,, presented by the central line and four lateral segments. The fanning ,111 at the bottom, recalling the position of the roots, contains a vertical , •ment which is the foreskin after its removal. The three segments hmning out below symbolize the roots of a tree, the saselu, that grew on lh · blood of the circumcision. The second figure , called "toy of the Fox's circumcision"39 (fig. 82), is i1 more realistic representation of the operation: the four sex souls of the ,wmmo semu are positioned in a bundle forming the trunk of the kilena, 1he "roots" of which fan out at the bottom. Ogo (in the form of the Fox) 1s bound on his back; the nommo titiyayne circumcises him with his 1eeth. Liberated by the nommo titiyayne, the sex souls then went near the ite where the purifying plant yayaga had grown from the blood of the emasculation of the nommo semi and "lay down there," awaiting their final assignment.

1t1-,ide an oval of about 40 cm. in diameter, the orbit of the po star. a ,11 c with ends slightly spiraling in opposite directions, symbolizes the II II and surrounds, on the one hand, the sun (a) situated at the periphery 11111 , on the other hand, Sirius (b) placed at one of the centers of the lltp e. Circle a has only three rays, the absence of the fourth recalling 1hl' removal of the foreskin, cut off by the fish's jaw and thrown into the 1111. Circle b has four rays, the feminine number, whereas the three of a 1 nnote masculinity. Thus, the quest is portrayed of the male (Ogo) 1 ,rning around the female (later to be Yasigui) without ever reaching h ·r. However, Ogo's virility has virtually passed into the fish, causing the tip of its tail to swell, thus underlining the fact that the fish had taken
back his sex souls.
The altar also denotes the presence in the ginna of the two kikinu say f sex of the sacrificed victim. Within the family dwelling. it will be the depository and guardian of those same two principles of the boys until their marriage. However, before they marry, they will undergo the same mutilation inflicted upon Ogo by the nommo titiyayne: circumcision. The child, until then considered to be androgynous, has his feminine aspect - the foreskin - removed by this operation which, at the same time, definitively determines his personality as a male: it is said of an

56. taba 10/0 1111um a/a nomoy beme kikin u gelie. amma sagu. u sag11. Th e opera1ion is performed al the third moon . 57. The yo ung Dogon woman lives in her father's hom e : she spends tbe night with her husband , but is still not officially recognized as his wife. At her first pregnancy , she bri n • to her husband's ginna some water drawn from the family pond, where the so uls of th unborn children live , and pours it into the jar in the main room . This gesture "legitimizes th e child she carries. ben she expects the third child. she is qualified to come and live in her husband 's home , an act which constitutes her acceptance into his ginna. At that time, the figure in question is drawn on the clay of the altar made of eartb from the family pond. After tbesacrifice consecrating the marriage. raw yu porridge is poured on the altar, and the family ea ts a di sh of rice, ara da, and drinks beer made from emme di giru. Then , each year, th e young woman offers a chicken. which the family head sacrifices in her name. The rule regarding the admission of the young woman at her third pregnancy has changed with time : presently , the rite is generally performed at the first or second pregnancy.

At Orosongo, the figure caJled "tonu of the circumcision of the Fox the nommo titiyayne in the star of the po" (fig. 90)0 1 is drawn on lh fayade of the sanctuary of the Manda totem. It is composed of a circl po tolo, which contains, on one side, the ereal grains and, on the oth side, Ogo who has just been circumcised by the fish, whose tail is now completely curled up in the direction of sigi tolo, Sirius, represented by a circle crossed by a diameter. His foreskin, transformed into the nay (Hemitheconyx caudicinctus), is in the placenta/ sun, which is represented by a sort of racquet. . J \' THIRD AND FINAL DESCENT.
Il e r hi circ um i ion , Ogo went back down to Earth, unable to bring 11 thing with him. To do this, be used the o bject be had made with the 111 rvures" of the primordial egg and abandoned the first time he emerged lon111 Aroma' wom b. Thi instrumen t, which formed Ogo's third ark , merged through the ,p ·ning previously made , came down, and landed n the ground , 11hout spinning as its predecessors had done. T his absence of move,n ·nt m eant the end of Ogo's celestial journey and his final settling down o n Earth. It also showed that "the creation was finished." The third ark of Ogo's descent is presen lly r presented by the nugoro lms ket, o r "bean bonnet," made of saguba straw.65 Shaped like a bonnet, 11 is made of a spiral supported by segments or ''rays." Alone, incomplete , and ever rebellious, but , , l'. he will play a necessary part in the development of life on Earth. ,1 11 i said that Aroma provoked Ogo's actions in order to experiment 11h Lhe universe in formation: "Aroma made the Fox do all these things '" try him out in the world."1 Therefore, one sometimes adds that 111ma, angry at Ogo's reascent, reproached him for this unauthorized , I and forced him to go down again ''to show the way" for all the future
,I ·scents" of the beings reated in h.is womb.
The Fox had initiated agriculture, but by sowing stolen seeds - the ., •men" of his "father" Amma - inside the Earth , his ".mother." It will 1 • necessary to purify the soil, parched by incest, in order to renew its { rti\ity. To carry out this purification , men will sow in their turn, but with seeds given to them by Aroma for this purpose, and not with stolen unes. The Fox wi\l then leave to take refuge in the uncultivated bush, his domain. However. man will follow him and will again purify new terriLOry by delimiting new fields. Thus, the Fox's presence as well as his
actions will promote the expansion of mankind.
This idea of the expansion of the human domain, progressively impinging upon that of the Fox (who show the way), is illustrated or inaugurated (one may say) in children's games. The role of the very young children, who have a "joking relationship" with the Fox (manu), is to play

Therefore ii seems fr 11 i give certain commentaries regarding this method of divina1ii ,n technique of instru tion. Divination "by the Fox" is an activity intended sole ly fo r thl· Anyone who has reached maturity, if be wishes, may be instruckd ord ained: be is then called y urugu y ay ne, "be who goes (Lo Lbe ta hl the Fox,' or yurugu kunnone, "he who places (the tables) of the I Experienced diviner are always high initiates: beads of joint fanul digni taries of the Society of Masks , healers , hunters, e tc. Ho neither th e totemic priests, nor Lbe Hogon, who are respon ible for I community's Lribure to the adversary and conqueror of the Fox , n approach the tables: nevertheless, being initiates, they are inslructcJ everything that concerns them. A distinction is made between two diffe rent types of tables: the ac1u divination tables and the tables of instru tion, used for teaching t initiates and future diviners. They are cailed kala, "to tear ," as Lbey a related to the placenta "tom o ut'' by Ogo and to the cosmic consequem: of this act. The round table, called kala gono, ' Lorn rounded," is the fi r of mythical time· it is no longer used, but was ritually drawn near th cave o f Ka at the tjme when the Dogon arrived. We have seen that , fro the poin t of view of Lhe worlds creation by Amma, it represen ts th division of the primord ial placenta inlo parcels; with regard Lo the Fo it is the image of the nortbeastem "hole" be made in his tom-out placenta '·spread out lik e Earth " the place where the stolen po will germinate The explanations of the symbolism of this table are part of the instru tjon given to the initiates. A ) T he commonly used divination table is rectangular and is called kala sibe nay ,"to tear at the four co rners. ' It represents the second piece ''torn out" by Ogo. The rectangle is divided in half lengthwise and into three parts widthwise, th us having six compartments: if the round table is the image of th e firs t •fi eld ,'' or the field of the p o , the rectangular table is the image of a fie ld in six parcels, as established by the Fox to sow seeds in after the desc n t of the ark and of man - an attempt that will end in fai lure.87 The diviner traces the basic fig ures on this table - which is not

the order of the basic figu res (A,B ,C .D E ,F) c nstitutes an 111 "Ogo's walk" in space namely . his successive "descen ts" and r • between sky and Earth, the third of which leads to his death. I{ · the theor tic al zigzag line which then connects the registers 1111 "The path of death is crooked , the path of life is straight, tht· p death is three."8H The Fox descended from the sky to the Earth which he form ·J seeded : when he moves about on the divination tables, "thl' pi begins to turn as if Crom the movement of his paws." T he appointed diviner smo ths the table with the "ba nd of the I y urugu numo, made of sene wood . The apprentice diviner may use c one stone "hand ," a sort of triangular pebble with which to draw registers. This pebble, heavier than wood , symbolizes bo th the plal· a heavy thing stolen by the Fox, and the responsibil ity , heavy a. I stone, taken on by the apprentice. T he stone "hands" ar "stolen" by the diviners from the terrace of l Lebe dala which symbolizes the '·sky,' the place where the events inv1•I ing the Fox and his twin br th rs took place , especially the redeenu acrifice of the nommo semu. 89 T he o bject's point represents the sun, an its fla t side , with which one smooths the tables, i the sky in which moves (fig. 14); what is drawn on the raised part with the flat sill reproduces an image of the sky on the ground. Moving the stone mean to "lay" the sky and sun upon the Earth. When the apprentice i ordained , he offers two chickens for sacrifice on the altar to the Fox. The eldest diviner present consecrates this by taking his hand and having him smooth the table with the sene "smoother." The altar called "Le be of the Fox" (yurugu Lebe) is erected near the place where the first divination table will be drawn as established by the community's diviners. The name of the altar, Le be, is a contraction of fey be, "those two," or "two together" ; it expresses the status of the one to whom it is consecrated , who is "both Ogo and the Fox." From a mythical perspective, the yurugu Lebe will be consecrated by men after the death

3. SACRIFICE OF THE NOMMO
Slaughter of rhe victim. Division of !he victim 's body. Projection of the pans of the body into space.
Jn circumcising Ogo, the sacrificer had acted too hastily, on his own , 1111I without Amma's orders. Amma said that he "had not waited" for hat be was going to do. For had Ogo no't been circumcised, Amma 1111ply would have destroyed him to prevent him, o nce and for all, from 1 ,tllsing any harm. However, this deed was so hastily committed that it 111ade Ogo's blood penetrate the celestial placenta as well as his own placenta, the Earth, upon which he had also bled. A purification of the ,' ·lestial and terrestrial spaces then became necessary.

1 •,t lhe clavicles intact and pure in the living being, and to avoid 1 h I h tween it and the body he was going to cut up. One says that , anting to keep the Nommo's name from being lost, left 111111• whole in his body." 1111i1 then proce ed to work on the Nommo's body, repeating the 1 c ,;tages of the formation of the primordial germ , the po seed. , 111 that the po - in which Amma had reintegrated the four 111, first contained by the seed of the sene - had grown around a 1 1 11 l' re in seven vibrations, or "words," composing its life within. 'l'hlh, belonging to the "sex" and foreshowing the seed's germina1 h,td manifested itself as an external vibration, from which future , 1111~ • of reproduction were to be born. I •~ ,ng the body of the sacrificed Nommo, Amma resumed his work t II same way as before. The contents of the clavicles, seat of the eight , 11 ds" in their life-giving stage, had flowed out with the blood. Turn. the victim on his back, he had the body opened, first lengthwise, h 11 widthwise , producing f ur counterparts of the four elements, kize 1,11· When it was open , he took out the seven internal organs through lu ·h, by way of the blo d , the already differentiated and individual' , rl ·•word ' was circulating. Just as he had put aside for safekeeping the , 1111ents of the sex organ, seat of the eighth word, so he also preserved 1th the organs, the seven others with the intention of returning them in 1h ·ir entirety to the Nommo at the time of his resurrection. Thus, in 11rder to place them in a regenerated universe, along with the Nommo's other internal organs, Amma took back the sources of the life, devel11ped in seven vibrations inside the primordial germ - the po - to which was added the eighth vibration, evidence of its germination. First he took the liver, kinne na, which is the "seat of the heart" ;132 it ,Jlso is the privileged seat for the being's life force, nyama, which circulates with the blood in the veins. Demonstrating the "force" conr.ained by the organ (the liver), one cuts a bit off to throw it on the altar during a sacrifice, saying: "The grilled liver we throw is like the exterior word (that is) coming out and being thrown."133 The liver is made up of

Th later to be used by man for a series of purification rites. From the gall bladder, gala dimme or "curved bitter," and thu the liver, emerged the pelu which, together with the kilena, will h instrument for the resurrection performed by Amma. From the heart emerged the tuber of the kinu bommo, called 111 (literally ''perpetual"); this incorruptible bulb, proof of the immo heart beats of the resurrected Nommo, was to be put in the ground d url the fou nd ation of a ginna to e nsure the permanence of the life of I co mmunity. 146 The ezegele was born from the pancreas; the satele from the spll' the saguba from the lungs; the sana from the intestines; finally, a full of millet, y u (or sayu), was born from the kidneys, the seat of "birth Generally speaking, this plant, which dies and is reborn from its wn seed, will be like a living image of the principle of resurrection. Eacho the plants related to the victim's organs that we have just named a l. 1 corresponds to one of the eight articulations of the "word." On Earth they will become the special instruments of purification, dolu, needed by man in case of violation of a prohibition. The effect of these purific.i tions is to reestablish in the body of the offender the stability of those principles which were disrupted by his offense. In regard to the first mythical generation, the eight ancestors humanity are primarily considered as forming a single clan, all origin ing, therefore, from one totem. The clan will be "divided" into five at t third generation , then into seventeen at the fifth, in relation to th development of the mythical "families." All together, the twenty-two fi "great totems," binu na, theoretically constitute the symbol of I collective worship rendered by all men of all clans to the sacrific Nommo. It is said that "the woman who 1111 birth, the placenta remains for seven (weeks) in the place whl'1 will wash themselves; (the child) it is (there) as if (it were) living i11 it is as if it were living in water ; it is as if it were living in the water w11 Nommo."217 Indeed, the placenta and attached umbilical cord an· f1 a piece of pottery which is placed in the courtyard under the dun composed of millet left to rot there. With regard to integrity of any placenta, a being or an object which is never suscepll to impurity is called kize mene, " thing (in) the placenta." REPRESENTATIONS.
I. With regard to geographic representation, the Dogon haw the sacrifice and resurrection of the Nommo in the Songo re).! 111 have, to this effect, established (ritual) sites in the mountain ran , tower above it (Map 1).244 Two large paintings have been made on the rocks which overhan great vault. The first one is red and white and is called ·'trace u sacrificed Nommo's blood" (nommo semi illi bummo). It recall flowing blood, the red being the "clear blood" (serum) and the "the oil" (plasma). The second one is called "drawing of the st111 (place) of the resurrected Nommo" (nommo bulo inu toy). "Thl' form (of rock) is several meters above ground level and overlool.. entire neighboring region. Because of this, the great painting on ceiling of the rock that juts out seems to show itself to the enl surrounding countryside."245 (Pl. IX, 1 and 2). The drawings on the great vault (about 30m. long and 2m. hight called ogodine tonu, "tonu of the chameleon."246 For all initiates th pictures, which are sometimes trichromatic, represent Aroma's signs - at the stage of the "variegated drawing" (in color): 'The to11u the chameleon, one to one, are like the 266 words."247 As in the figur the Nommo, the three colors that have been used denote: black, "l world''; red, "the life of the world"; white, "the word and the water." 1IfE TO TEMIC CLAN.
I) The resurrected Nommo is represented by anthropomorphic statu' 1tcs of varying sizes that are part of the material in the totemic sanctu,11 ic . They are carved in the wood of a plant from the category - or 1,mily - called kuno (of which the first is teguzu), 275 considered to be
,1fe from any risk of impurity. ach statuette embodies one aspect of the Nommo's functions, as well as his attitude toward his creator, Amma. Each of them is more
partic ularly associated with one of the totems.
If the statuette has one arm raised above its head, it represents the Nommo ent rusting himself to his creator before his sacrifice, in anticipation of the events to come. He is saying: "Only one Amma: 1am with mma."276 His gesture also indicates his future role as organizer and his place in the center of the universe; o ne says of him: "His raised arm
hows the middle of the world ."277 (Pl. XV II, 1). When both arms are raised, but apart. he is asking Amma to keep him near him after the resurrection, "like a child who holds his arms out to Ihis father": "The Nommo Amma slaughtered and brought back to life, hi , arms are raised, so that Aroma can take him." 278 On the statuette hown on Plate XVII, 2, the necklace represents the water, the dugoy, ,ind the cowries; the crisscross rectangl e on his abdomen is the ark in which he will descend to Earth and its compartments as well as the
1uaring off of the cultivated soil.

VAL UE AND FUNC TION OF THE SACRIFICE AND
RESURRECTION OF THE NOMMO.
The Nommo's sacrifice and resurrection constitu te one of the ru
mental tages in the building and organization of the second Ulll \
created by Amma.
Examined in their entirety as a system of represe ntation, th e lll'I morphology of the emasculation, sacrifice, and resu rrection; th •
form ance of the operations; and the mythical place of their execu1 io11
establish specific relations between: - the Nommo's placenta, - the Nommo's body and vita l organs, - th e stars born of the sacrificial blood, - some plants and animals fo rmed at the same time - ~an, who will be created from the matter of hi placenta. Trus et of relations reveals a series of parallei structures: - an organization of space - stars in motion - and of tim
c a le n d a rs,
- ~ s cial organization - at the various levels of the tribe, 1 tote~c clan ,_ and the lineage - and corresponding cults,
. m r lat100 wi_th th~s~ fi rst two structures, all ritual aspects agnculture. the bas,c act1v1ty of the Dogon society.
For man as an individual, the sacrifice and resurrection signify 1 devel~pment of a b ing from tb e fe tal to the adult stage. This deve lt rnent mclud~s the ~volu tio n of the child who, until circumcision (m· I or f~~ale) rs considered to be of ei ther sex - th e nommo anagonn sacr_,fice~ as an androgyne · then, after the operation, the child full realizes its masculine or feminine being - the mixed couple of resu rected Nommo. The formation of the unum is first recalled by a figure of eighty (8xl yala; this number emphasizes the reproductive role of the mythi ancestors. THE CLA VJCLE.
The primary place given to the clavicle in the formation of the hu being emphasizes its importance. It is said that "the principal 1d1
bone of the Nommo (is) the bone of the clavicle. Amma began tht· n bone of the body of the fetus with the clavicle and the skull. An made the bone of the clavicle first. The rest of the bones he ma<l suspending them from it. The maturing (aging) of the clavicle (c ntinues) until he becomes a young man . 300 Indeed , for the Dogon : 1) The c lavicle is the suspension system for the entire skeleton. ( says th at ·' th clavicle is the means of suspen ion of th e body.''301 1 clavicl becomes linked to the entir sk ele ton when one is a bout twcn two years of age, the marriageable age amo ng the Dagon. 2) The clavicle contains the symb I of the eight primordial seeds. fact, the clavicle bone is called ani guyo ki, "granary bone of the a 11 and its hollow is called ani guyo. The whole thing is compared to granary ; the bone, being the fram ewo rk of the granary, contains t seeds. It is said that "the seeds emerged fro m the bone of the clavicles."
300. nommo ki vomo ku·go ani guyo ki; ani guy o ki-le kutogolo-le amma i noni vog11 gozu ki tolo ze. amma ani guy o ki la yegera ki vazu·go vogo·ne du/a y ege,u; ani guy o kipedo saga-rara-go ba. 301. ani guyo gozu de mme gogie vo.
302. ani guy o ki den e vogo·ne goy . The impo rtant seeds for ritual sowing are preserv hanging against the earthen walls of the gran ry. Similarly, the gourd seeds, gono (fro gono: "to take away" or "to come o ut of' (the clavicle)), especially in the past , were put in the walls of the ho uses, wh ich symboli ze the "clavicles" of the dwelling. When one so the seeds stored in this manner they appear to have •· merged" from a clavicle. II a wild or domestic animal is killed. its clavicles are preserved and hung up in th house. A bit o f th e bone is crushed and mixed with the seeds to increase the harvest.

THE ANAGONNO BILE AND THE ANAGONNO SALA.
T he two figures are also sketche of fishes, the anagonno bi/,· I 131), i.e. , the image of lhe human fetus. Indeed Lhe child wiU devcl11 its mother's womb and will pass from the fish stage to the stagl' ,1 hu man being, equipped with limbs enabling it to move about on I ,111 T his concept of th development of a fetus is expressed throu •I series of images which depict, after the creation of the clavicle. 1 formation of the anagonno bile, its transformation into lhe afla~11111t sala, then into a human b ing or "person ," inne. As in the mall·n womb, on the mythical level this metamorphosis will take place 1 time. It is said: "T he fo ur unum (sons of the fa ther) (who) came 0111 the sky with o nommo, they all descended a anagonno bile, landed l
Ea rlh as anagonno sala, and wh n they left the ark to walk on the earl
they be ame person s."3 11
The term bile may be translated as "is going to transform itself" an signifies the beginning of a metamorphosis. The anagonno bile become the anagonno ala r "ordinary anagormo," i.e., the Clan senegalensis. Particular charac teristics distinguish lhis fish 's body fro the nommo anagonno. The anagonno bile has a narrow r h ad anJ fatter body than the nommo anagonno. The anagonno sala i , in th regard , compara ble to the anagonno bile; its tail, however, opens i111 two parts which prefigure the two legs of ma n.
The two tonu of the anagonno bile and the anagonno sala show rh development of the fetus (fig. 131 and 132) . The first is drawn in r ochre, bana, in the place where wom en who are menstruating wash. themselves during their isolation. The second i drawn with p rridge yu and ara geu on a boulder or stone brought there for this purpos called "stone of the week ,' dugu dummo, on which a woman who. menses are over comes to sit down on the sixth day after she hu performed her final ablutions. There , she drinks a pulp of raw millet and returns to her husband 's home after the evening meal.
Like the nommo anagonno, the anagonno bile is without any joint. articulations; he does , however , have a sort of crest, called izu ginen,•

Ill. - Amma then m deled the Fox's femal e twin, Yasigui , using the nctim's placenta , but at the place where blood from Ogo's c irc umcision lrnd flowed. Made from elements taken fro m the two' first twins," she also belongs 1n Lhe generation of the bau. "Y asigu i was molded fro m the placenta and lhe male (blood) (of the Fox)."340 She will descend to Earth , in her turn, m human form during the first eclipse of the sun. This recalls the origin of her spiritual principles: they came from the part of Ogo's placenta 1hat bad been transformed into the sun. IV. - One sometimes says derisively that Amma used the rest of the Fox's umbilical cord to create the shoemaker, dau (from da, ·'separate"); 341 his physiological status makes him a basically impure being,puru. Like lhe Fox, of whom he is a "sort of twin," he will receive, in his clavicles, the po banu, made impure by the creator of disorder. One says that "the shoemaker can open people's navel, the key to the belly, to take the seed s"; for if one drinks at the same time as a shoemaker does, the N mmo takes back the nyama of the seeds that are in the clavicles.342
DEA TH: THE ANAGONNO ALA G ALA.
Aroma created man immortal, as originally the nommo anagonno had been which came from his womb. However, the misdeeds of the Fox and the ensuing impurity they communicated to the Earth will bring about a eries of disorderly events which will result in the appearance of death. lL will afflict all living beings: it will be mitigated, to a certain extent by the funeral rites, the purpose of which is to regroup the deceased's spiritual fo rces, which have been released and dispersed by the suppression of life and the decay of the body. This study of man would not be complete if, in view of the events to follow, the representations dealing with the status and functions of the dead were not commented upon here. When a man dies, he becomes an anagonno fish again, that is to say, he resumes his primitive fetal form. The deceased is carried to the cemetery wrapped down to his ankles in the family "funeral btanket": the two

I he "gree tings of the bulu " begin with visits called ginna y anu made I•\ !he members of a ginna, first to their own patriarch, then to the other 1111 mbers of the lineage. T he entire group o f the people visited corresp,111ds to the thirty first y ala. I• r exam pi , Ambara goes to pay a visit to the ginna bana of Amtaba , ,,I Gu.inna, and of Sodamma;69 then he goes lo all his "brothers ," in the l11oudest sense of the term, originating from his ginna. The next visits are called togu yanu, or "greetings to tbe family" (also 111 the broadest sense): everyone visits peo ple from ho use to house , where one drinks the beer that is always offered. These visits co rrep nd to the second group of thirty yala. Then come the ama yanu, or 'greetings to the pa rents-in-law," which torrespond to the 3 (+3) y ala represented by the father-in-law , the mothe r-in-law , and their daughter. By its spinning the unwound po had poured everything it vnn l into the Nommo's "great ark ,' or ko ro na. Thi ark was matk lr11 rest of the victim's placenta . Now this placenta, unlike that ol" I>, not unde rgone th e tearing and transformations which had , l'rl affected the piece that formed th e Earth and had rendered it 1111 The Fox's ark , made of "impure" earth , is the symbol o f thl' 1111 vated bush; the Nommo's ark, made of 'p ure earth," will desCl'llll e erything Am.ma created and is sym bolic of the cultivated earth II be called izubay minne, "eart h o f the day of the fish" - a term emphasizes its origin , its ties with the sacrificed Nommo, and human beings, bis descend ru1ts, who will keep their original fet a! It This "earth of the fish" will progressively extend itself over th · I earth in proportion to the development of agriculture which, itsd f, constitute a purification of the ground made impure by theft and iul Theoretically, the ark had as many compartments as the numh • holes the Fox had dug in the gro und: sixty, this number bein!! I "reckoning of the placenta ," me lugi. With regard to the social structure and technical development . rh sixty compartments of the ark were also related to the theoret 1 divisio n o f the primal family field between the four eponymous am: tors , a field they were to constitute and which will be like ''plated" on I< of the earth of the Fox. From this per pective, the distribution of th compartments is in fo ur rows of fifteen , with 3 x 5 lots per ancestor (fi 153).1 Symbolically peaking , th e num bers 3 (attribu ted to the male) an 5 (attributed to the generatio ns) togeth er represent the patrilinear Jin of descent. It is said : "Th sixty holes o f th e ginna minne are divided into five , five ."2 T his termino logy is also rela ted lo the fi ve finge rs of th band that will seed the field .

1 r>NTENTS OF THE ARK.
l he ark was soft and moist like the placenta it was made of. It held all 111,· things and beings created by Am.ma and later poured out by the 11male po pilu. Its structure and con tents are evidence of the Nommo's IH 'I victory won ov r the Fox. In comparison to Ogo' fir t two arks , ,,nsidering their narrowness and their essential, but meager contents, 1 well as to his third , a stru cture of lattice-work that held nothing, the Nommo's ark was unique, for it was fu ll. When one says that the "sixty holes of the ark are like the holes of the ant," it is because one com pares the structure to an ant hill full of these innumerable, active insects. The Nommo's ark embod ied the role Aroma had conferred on him : to organize, direc t, and control the whole created universe. On the ark were, first of all, the resurrected Nommo and the eight ancestors , or four couples of twi ns. Each one of them was related to one

T his figure is called '"drawing of th sirige file site o f th e ark that desc nded upon Earth and that s rved as a 111,111 ·I for the building of houses"4 ' (D). In Lhi case each of the plain 1, , 1ungles of the mast recalls a dwelling · nd its storeroom or kana 111tl luding he ginna, individ ual h use, and sanctuary). T he mask it elf "' ,v have two statuettes mounted on th top , representing the couple o ( , urrected Nommo, mankind's ancestors. A. tuaJly, this image of the sirige as a "storied house" stems directly fwm certain elements in the architecture of the ginna. Like the sirige, th · fa9ade of the dwelling denotes b th the ark and its descent, but also hows the human con tents of the structu re . A certain number o f nich es Ii tve bee n made in the lay which are euphemistically called ·'swallows' l111les" (vevele bonno). Above the ntrance door opening ont the ball 1wo simila r niches place the resu rrected Nommo and h is female twi n in lhc center. On either side of the door, four vertical rows of ten niches wmbolize the lineages and the generations descendent from th e eight ,tncestors, which are themselves represented by the eight ga bl s rising ,hove the fai;:ade. The granary on the top floor, called dele (literally : \uspended"), is the ark in the sky: on the door are carved figure called 'raised arms ," being the ancestors of the lineage , whose gesture invokes the protection of the eight ancestors over the millet in the granary. The entrance door. through which the living pass, is the ark on Earth. When the dwelling is consecrated. one prays to Amma that the "threshold be soi led" by the numerous footprints of the members of the li neage. ln this way, the entire far;ade expresses both the descent of the ark and the succession of generations that "descend" from the mythical an cestors it carried.

After the Nommo , all Lhe beings aboard the ark , in their tu rn , desce nd, don the Ea rth. When iL was co mplete ly empty, Ammapulled the chain 1ha l bad sup ported it up to the sky again, then he "closed off" the sky. When the men, who bad seen sigi lolo shining during the descent, l11nded , they were witness to th e fi rst rising of the sun, which came o ut of !he east and which, from then on , illuminated the universe: it was then in ifs equinoctial position, called "sun of the middle," nay logoron, correspo nding to springtime. Its rising an d setting , its movements in the course 11( the day will, from that time o n, testify to the arrival of th e a rk and to the Nommo's presence on the Earth of the Fox. 3) In the totemic sanctuary, the rectangular platter calkJ ,.,,, usually made of ga guyo wood, represen ts the "ark in the wall , was pull ed into it by the Nommo transfo nned into a horse. ·1h taot sanc tuaries sometimes contain several arks, whose form 111 bolism re present vario us episodes of the myth or ye t various al 11,hu of the Nommo. To illustrate this, we present below the analv,1 1 platters studied in the sanctuary of the Manda totem of I h , (which have all been the subject of commentaries) in the 111111 o rd er: A) This first one (fig. 167A), also the largest, is a sort of ho roughly semicylindrical bottom and two large handles. A cup-Ii i.. is hewn near one handle to recei\'e the victim's blood. There a, knobs on either side of the vessel. These eight knobs denote tlw seeds and the eight ancestors. This box represents the ark comin)! ,,u the sky, filled with all things Aroma had placed in it , especially l grains whose spiritual principles are kept by the Nommo in thl' w 1 B) The second one has the appearance of a small trough with a I< central compartment and two small cups of the same depth to l'II side (fig. 167B). These three hollows sit on a somewhat wider, bottom, all one piece with the rest , from which jut eight knohs. object is consecrated to the "dry foods," and its symbolism expr •ss movement, namely, that of the "advance of the grains in the world." J the left small cup that belongs to the Nommo, he creates the grains, sends them out to the hollow on the right , where man will cull iv them. To get there , they cross the central hollow , the dry domain of I Fox: this is why people harvest them dry. C) The third (fig. 168) has the appearance of a rectangular trough, four legs shaped like truncated cones; one of its short sides has a row four points and the other has a handle in the shape of a hollow loze n crossed diagonally by the object's axis , which then protrudes a bit fro the lozenge. On each of the long sides is a frieze of twenty-two notch The object may be analyzed from two perspectives, either with regard to the Nommo, or with regard to the Fox: a) The usual position of the platter is vertical , the four horns on top symbolizing the cardinal points and the gables above the fa~ade of the sanctuary. The handle depicts the rainy and the clear sky; the diagonal is the "cord of rain " ; the lozenge is an outline of two rainbows joined al

(ll'Ctively, the three platters from Manda symbolize the sacred ,md the succession of the generations; the first is the "grandfa' uud the fie ld of U be ; the second is t he "father" and the field o f the 1, lhe third is the "son" and the fi eld of the binu. 1111 urk is al o represe nted by handicrafLs and a great many everyday I•, 1s or Loots , some of which recall the fo rm of the ritual platters. I I I he wooden drum, koro, represents the Nommo's ark . It is made of 11111i wood and has the same rectang ular shape as the koro bogu, 11 with a navel,' the image o f Ogo's rk and placenta, but without prot uberance on the sid e. Playing the instrument by alternately ,ii ,ng the sides and the bottom (i .e., the sky and the Earth) symbolizes I•scent o f the ark. Although the koro bogu is played in the bush by 1o , hildren guarding the fields while the millet grows , the koro is also r 1•J by children in the village as a sign of joy when the millet is 1111ifully ripe· for the sound of the wooden drums , good for all , 111kind, is also good for the growth of the cereal grains. Through its 1 ·s, the mi llet "hear the sound" of the instru ment which , like rain in 111 air penetrates it through the leaves. One says of the sound of the "'o: "The voice (sound ) and the rain, the tw (are) one; the v ice that I rIhe millet) c atches, is the voic fa g ntle heart."68 One als says that 111 li fe force ny ama, reaches and p netrate Lhe in trument by the beat 11I fhe stick used to play it with ; from there , it travels with the sound and t, ,ches the millet. The koro i also played at harvest Lime. 1) The ark is also represented by the rectangular wood en box for the , 11ne of i (fig. 169).69 T raditionally, it is composed of two parallel rows of 1•hl cup-holes carved into the wood , with two larger cup-holes at each end; 1It · whole thing represents the ark in the sky. T he bottom o( the case - the I irrh - has eight small protuberances or knobs carved in to the main 111 ·ce, which are the mythical ancestors. During the game , the object is oriented from east to west, like the ark , hen it landed ; one of the rows of hole represen ts the women , tbe other rhc men. The game sym bolizes the union of tbe sexes and the p rolifera tion

"The Fox s ark descended, the Nommo· gr eat ark. descended , they 1lc ·cended to the north near the Niger. Amma Serou, Lebe Serou , Binou ~crou, and Dyo ngou Serou, who wa with the Nommo , all descended in the ark at Dyigou. The Dogon came from Lhe northeast.''79 The wide land-d pression of the Debo is flooded when the Niger's \ aters rise (fig. 173). At the lake's entrance to the southeast are rocky nnd often stee p hills of varyi ng size overlo k ing Lhe entire region , ome 11f which form islands when the waLers are at their maxjmum height. On th largest of these, Gou rao, there are three villages inhabited respec1ively by the Bozo , the Malinke, and the Fulbe. A Bozo village is located 111 the a rthem tip of Sori ba (Mou nt Saint-C harles). The oth er two, the Rock of Marie-T h ' re and Mount Sainl-Henri. are smaller in size and unha bited. T he "boulders" of this region of the lake are evidence of the events we have related : "The boulders of Debo are the seat o f the Norum 's descended ark. "xo G o urao rep resen ts the descent of the ark: "The boulder of Gourao is like the sky fro m which the vessel emerged and descended."xt At its summi t, a tab le of rock - the ark - urrounded by raised stones - th e stars - represents the descent in th e sky. The bado bulu rites belong to the Arou in the sense that the Dyon can celebrate the m only when the Arou have assigned the date. On the other hand, the Dyon hegin the buw • rites and the others follow. The Ono decide upon the date of the agu rite (from ay, to get or catch) performed at nay banu at the spring equinox following the hunt ta/a (cf. infra. p. 282). In the past , agu (which involved the altars of the ancesiors, vageu) was celebrati:d among the Ono well before the bulu of the Arou. The Donno performed a ritual <lane~· accompanied by wrestling matches, adori, hypothetically performed on the field of Lehc: this ritual game is called koro boy go. "dance to the beat of the koro (drum),'' by the inhahitants of Sanga. Similarly, in Sanga, the four "measures" of the sun detennine the performance of the following rites: goru at the winter solstice (sun of the south); sacrifice to the ommolo ana of the ginna (ginna ommo/o bu/u) at the spring equinox (sun of the middle): bulu, the sowing celebration at the summer solstice (sun of the north): "beer of the twins." dineu kono (offering of the first fruits) at the autumn equinox (sun of the middle). 133. d,ma to/o yazu dimmia nay gono dege dege.

From tlm perspective, they constitute an agricultural calendar , called dine hin · "work of the winter season," which has the following nomenclature:
enegirim: sowing and kinu tumu (germination), "nose that is coming out" , dige tanu: emerged from the earth, or kinu tey, ''grown nose"; donno tolo : millet grown into an ear , or yu ene du/lo, "chicken-tail millet"; yazu or bayara: grains formed in the ear, or "pregnant millet," yu bere t1y ; obia: "ripe millet," yu ilfa; yapunu da: consumed millet (hence, "dead," yu yimu), but then present in the clavicles of the people it has nourished.

follows 1he order of t.he position oi Venus. the y ala should be rea as follows (fig. 182): - the first position , obia, has fifteen y ala (a). The east-west orientation of the seven ya/a recalls that everything begins in the east (du, "root' ). T he figure connotes the in ternal development of the primordial po at first seven segmen ts, then the ej ction of the eigh th). the creati n of the no m mo anagonno, and the future births of mankind (ku no a nd twi ns (7 + 8))_13K - the second position donno tolo, has sev n ala (b), which connote the division of the body of the sacrificed Nommo and the distribution of the seeds in space. - the third position ,yazu, has four yala fo r the ear a nd two dashes for the stalk - six mark in all (c) - and re presents the mi llet ear calledyu binukezine, 139 which is kept in the granary with the seeds where, opening into four , it represents Amma's four clavicles, the f ur elements, and the four cardinal directions. - the fourth position, enegirim, has nine yala, "the eight seeds and the calabash seed is nin th " (d ), which represent all the cereal grains in the contain r, gaba. - the fifth position, dige tanu, has sixteenya/a (e); these a re the seeds given by Amma to the Smith, who will bri ng them down in his hammer to give them to man. - the sixth position,yapunu da, has eightyala (f) which represent the eight seeds of the clavicles that the eight ancestors of humanity will receive after the first harvest and the consumption of the cereal grains on Earth.Ho If one adds up the yala of the six positions, fifty-nine in all, plus the planet itself, it makes a total of sixty, in other words, the "number of the placenta."

AMMA CLOSES HIMSELF AGAIN
Once the signs were in place, Amma closed himseU again, keeping to himself one "half' of the life o{ the world. The clavicles folded themselves up and the whole thing again look on the initial egg-shape: but, on the outside, four lines of separation cou ld be see n recalling the opening. This final stage of Aro ma's work is represented by a figu re called ·'drawing of Aroma who has sat down and closed up after he had fini bed molding the world ,"23 which is drawn facing east on th square block of stone placed on the spot called amma doy , beneath which the diagrams of the 266 primordial "signs" have been drawn.24 It is drawn with porridge made of fo ur cereal grains (po p ilu, emme ya, yu a nd ara) that are proof of the fu ndamental presence of the fo ur elements and it marks

Amma gave almost all his powers to the Nommo, his depu ties, to whom he nt rusted th e progress a nd growth of the universe a nd the care of living ~rea tures: the nommo die, who resides with him in the sky, is the guar~1an ~f the spiritual pri nciples of food plants especially of the eigh t pnmord1al seeds; the nommo titiyayne carries out th e latter's orders and serves as an intermediary: o nommo lives in the waters on the Earth , where he holds the spirituaJ p ri nciples of people, his descend• ants. Amma will be invoked by people in a ll situations and in all their pra~e~s, ~ut , fo r the mosl part, he will not intervene direc tly ; when a sacnfice 1s offered on the a ltars bearing his name , his deputies will be called upon , for they have - by his will - the power to act. He retains o~ly th~ duty of tak ing care of the dead, since death is later to appear in this umverse, particularly on the Earth of the Fox. The whole life of this second creation is present in "Amma's egg": its guar_antee li~s within the twenty-two signs inscribed in the closed-up clav1cles, which thus remain in the womb of the creator. Therefore, the closed egg of Amma is called "egg of Amma guardian of the world."37 After every sixty-year ceremony of the Sigui celebrating the end of one cycle and the beginning of another (sixty being the so-called "number of the placenta") , the participants purify their bodies with

A ppare ntly, the animal never goes to t.he edge of ponds; it d rin ks water from p uddles in th e r c ks . It is beli ved th a t it goes almost comple tely without water du.ri ng the dr y season. MAN'S CLAV ICLE
Tl'STUT. L. - Traite d"anatomie humaine. tome I. G. Do in et cie . ed ite urs, P aris, 1948.
Th e cl avic le is a long bone d ev loped as a pair and consequently non·symmetrical, transversa ll y ex tended, like a buttress, be tween th e top of the stern um and the scapula. Shaped like a n italic 5. it has t wo d istinct curvatures; an in ternal curvat ure with a concavity dire cted to wards the back an d un external curvatu re with a concavity dire cted 10 the front. M o reover, it is flat from top to bottom . thus offering fo r o bservation two sides. two edges , and two extremities p. 310). T he develo pme nt of the clavicle starts from rwo points of ossification . a primary and secondary center.
a) Prima,y center: It is intended fo r Lhe body and th e ex ternal ex tre mity and appears towards the end of the fourth week: itis the fi rst bone to appear on the skelet o n. It de velops at the point corresponding to the middl e of th fu ture clavicle a nd from there rapidly radiates to the extremities. The clavicle differs from th e other bo nes of the limbs in that it is not preceded by an outline of cartilage . Th e bony tissu e whi c h constitutes t he primitive c e nter mentioned above indeed begins amidst undifferentiated tissue and first grows at th e expense of this tissue. Later , two small growths of cartilage appear o n e ither side , which , as th ey lengthen de termine th e le ngth o f th e bo ne . and os~-ify in the ir turn. This mod e of developme nt is pec uliar to th e clavicle and is clearly ex plain ed by comparative anatomy. Among a gr eat number of lower ve rtebrates, partic ularly among fish . th e clavicle is a c omplet ely c utaneous and superficial bon e. A mong higher ve rtebrates, it reaches deeper levels and becomes connected 10 the skeleton: then, added to its tissue-lik e o utline will be an o utline of cartilage which ossifies, as do all parts o f the

I am delighted that the Continuum Foundation has sponsored the lranslation and publication in English of Le renard pale (The Pale Fox). a model of patient and sensiti ve anthropological fieldwork. Actually, the book owes as much to the Dagon themselves as to the two great French anthropologists. It illustrates beautifully. I think, the
scope and coherence of a so-called ''primitive," non-Western system f thought, and for that reason especially I am happy it will now be more accessible to students and, for that matter, to the general public.
- Mircea Eliade, Professor Emeritus of the History of Religions in the Divinity School of the University of Chicago.
'he Pale Fox is at !as · hed in English; it is a key link rn science and t ric. In the past ,

But now the g: Griaule en recorded the ancestral wor scribing th anion of Sirius, not realizing invisible hrough a large telescope. We have shows evid

Marcel riaule's Convers r'fft Ogoter eli introduced the religious ide · of the Dagon of the West A;,i Republic of Mali . In The Pale the renowned anthropolo · s Griaule and Dieterlen
journey dee and touch the cor· of thjt ogon's most sacred knowledge - revea · · n enigma riv...~ . of the Great Pyramid in the controversy it ha . The • . that the Dagon know the "secrets of the dark stars" - the lnvtstbllM1J11te dwarf and dwarf nova companions of Sirius.
Moreover, they point out, the Dogan know a host of astronomical facts (some of which cannot be discovered with the naked eye), possess an extensive anatomical and physiological knowledge, a wide-ranging pharmacopoeia, a 266-symbol written language, and know the nature and purpose of human existence. How the Dagon could have known all this is for some a "serious mystery." But if we go beyond "left-brain" modalities, as they did, and explore the borderlands of science where myth becomes realitv ., reality myth , where the dance of the unseen becomes illumin
rhythms of the seen, and where the spiritual realm is an integr'
material being - who knows what we might not discover!
- Hunter Havelin Adams III, Argonne National Laboratory.
